No Liability or Reward if Slave Runs from Retriever
Ruling on liability and reward for retriever of runaway slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability and reward for a retriever of a runaway slave when the slave escapes without his fault, and identify the evidence from the athar of Ali.

Core Idea
No liability without transgression; no reward without return.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario in Hanafi fiqh: a person finds a runaway slave but the slave escapes before being returned to the master. The Hanafi school provides a clear ruling based on the authoritative opinion of Caliph Ali ibn Abi Talib. Understanding this ruling helps distinguish between situations where the finder is responsible and where he is not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Clearly state the legal question: What is the ruling if a person who has found a runaway slave loses him before returning him? The answer: no liability and no reward.
The act is the loss of the slave from the retriever's custody.
2
Locate the dalil
Identify the proof cited: the athar from Ali ibn Abi Talib where he said, 'He is not liable.' This is a qawl sahabi.
The hadith is recorded in al-Bayhaqi and Abd al-Razzaq.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'la yadmanuhu' directly negates liability. The Hanafi principle is that daman (liability) only arises from transgression (ta'addi), which is absent here.
The text also notes that if the slave runs away without the retriever's transgression, no liability.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Shuraih al-Qadi held the retriever liable even without fault. The Hanafi school follows Ali's view over Shuraih's.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf among early authorities.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi school prefers Ali's opinion because it aligns with the general principle that liability requires fault (ta'addi). The athar of Ali is considered stronger evidence.
The author states 'wa nahnu naqulu biqawli Ali' – we follow Ali's saying.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان ابق من الذي رده فلا شيء عليه ولا جُعل له [ اور اگر بھاگ گیا اس سے جو واپس لوٹارہا تھا تو اس پر کچھ نہیں ہے اور نہ اس کے لئے مزدوری ہے۔ جو آدمی غلام کو واپس لا رہا تھا اس کے ہاتھ سے بھی غلام بھاگ گیا اور واپس لانے والے کے بغیر تعدی کے بھاگ گیا تو اس پر غلام کا ضمان نہیں ہے۔ لیکن اس کو مزدوری بھی نہیں ملے گی ۔ کیونکہ اس نے واپس نہیں لایا تو مزدوری کیسی؟ اثر میں ہے عن علی في الرجل يجد الآبق فيأبق منه لا يضمنه وضمنه شريح ونحن نقول بقول على ان كان الآبق ابق من دون تعديه ( الف ) ( سنن بیہقی ، باب الجعالة ، ج سادس، ج ۳۳۰، نمبر ۱۲۱۲۷ / مصنف عبدالرزاق ، باب العبد الآبق یابق ممن اخذه ج ثامن ص ۲۰۹ نمبر ۱۴۹۱۵) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ واپس لانے والے سے بھاگ جائے تو اس پر ضمان نہیں ہے۔
And if the slave runs away from the one who returned him, then nothing is upon him and no reward for him. [And if the slave runs away from the one who was returning him, then he is not responsible for anything and he has no wage. The person who was bringing the slave, the slave ran away from his hand, and if the retriever did not commit any transgression, then he is not liable for the slave. But he also does not get any wage, because he did not return him, so how can he deserve a wage? There is an athar from Ali regarding a man who finds a runaway slave and it runs away from him: he is not liable. Shuraih made him liable. And we follow the saying of Ali, provided the slave ran away without the retriever's transgression. From this athar, it is known that if the slave runs away from the retriever, he is not liable.]
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage addresses the situation where the finder of a runaway slave loses the slave before returning him to the master. The ruling is clear: if the slave runs away from the retriever without any negligence or transgression (ta'addi) on the retriever's part, the retriever is not liable (no compensation due) and also receives no reward, since he did not actually return the slave. The author supports this with an athar from Ali ibn Abi Talib: the finder is not liable, contrary to the view of Shuraih (al-Qadi) who held him liable. The Hanafi school adopts the view of Ali, which is that the retriever is not liable unless he committed a transgression.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Liability or financial compensation.
جعل (ju'l): Reward or wage for returning the slave.
آبق (abiq): Runaway slave.
تعدي (ta'addi): Transgression, negligence, or fault.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) He is liable and gets no reward
B) He is not liable and gets no reward
C) He is not liable and gets the reward
D) He is liable and gets the reward

Key Takeaway
A retriever of a runaway slave is not liable and gets no reward if the slave escapes without his transgression, based on the athar of Ali.

Taking Witnesses When Finding a Runaway Slave
Recommendation to have witnesses to prove intent to return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on taking witnesses when finding a runaway slave and explain its purpose in securing the reward.

Core Idea
Witnesses protect your right to the reward for returning a runaway slave.

This passage addresses a practical situation: you find a runaway slave. The author advises that you should take witnesses to prove your intention to return the slave. This procedural step ensures you are entitled to the reward (ju'l) and avoids suspicion of theft or intent to keep the slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
Recognize the act being ruled upon: taking a runaway slave (al-abiq).
The finder is not the owner but a third party.
2
Identify the recommended action
The text states 'yambaghi an yushhida' — it is befitting to have witnesses at the time of taking.
This indicates istihbab (recommendation), not wujub (obligation).
3
Understand the reason
Witnesses prove that the finder intends to return the slave to the owner, not to keep him.
This protects against accusation and secures entitlement to the reward.
4
Note the consequence of omission
Without witnesses, the finder may be suspected of taking the slave for himself and thus forfeit the reward.
The reward is only for one who intends to return.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينبغي ان يشهد اذا اخذه انه يأخذ ليرد على صاحبه [ اور مناسب ہے کہ گواہ بنائے جب غلام کو لے کہ اس کو پکڑا ہے تا کہ اس کے مالک کو لوٹائے۔ گواہ بنانے سے یہ تہمت نہیں رہے گی کہ اس نے اپنے لئے پکڑا ہے۔ جس کی وجہ سے وہ مزدوری کا مستحق ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ اگر اپنے لئے پکڑا ہو تو پکڑنے والا مزدوری کا مستحق نہیں ہوگا۔
And it is appropriate that he should have witnesses when he takes him, that he takes him to return him to his owner. [And it is appropriate to make witnesses when he takes the slave that he has caught him in order to return him to his master. By making witnesses, this accusation will not remain that he caught him for himself. Because of which he becomes entitled to the wage. Because if he caught him for himself, then the catcher would not be entitled to the wage.]
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This is a procedural recommendation (yambaghi) for the person who finds a runaway slave. The author advises that the finder should take witnesses at the time of taking the slave, to prove that he intends to return him to the master. This protects him from suspicion that he intended to keep the slave, which would disqualify him from receiving the reward (ju'l). If he takes the slave for himself, he is not entitled to any reward. Taking witnesses safeguards his right to the reward.

Key Terms
يشهد (yushhidu): To have witnesses present and bear witness to the act of taking the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Taking witnesses when finding a runaway slave is mustahabb to prove intent to return, thereby securing the right to the reward.

Rulings on Reward for Returning a Runaway Slave
Presents rulings on ju'l for returning runaway slaves and a fiqh dispute on liability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the reward amount when the runaway slave's value is below 40 dirhams, explain the condition for the finder's non-liability if the slave runs away again, and identify the Hanafi reliance on the legal opinion of Ali (r) over Shuraih.

Core Idea
A finder of a runaway slave is entitled to a reward of value minus one dirham, but is not liable if the slave runs away again without his fault.

This passage from Kitab al-Ibaq addresses several practical issues that arise when someone finds and returns a runaway slave. The basic rule is that the finder deserves a reward (juʿl), but the amount is adjusted to avoid the owner paying more than the slave's value. However, if the slave escapes again without the finder's negligence, the finder bears no liability—a ruling based on the authoritative opinion of Ali (r). Additionally, the text recommends taking witnesses to avoid suspicion, and specifies that if the slave is mortgaged, the mortgagee is liable for the reward.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reward rule for slaves valued under 40 dirhams
The text states: 'If his value is less than forty dirhams, he is awarded his value minus one dirham.' This is the Hanafi position to ensure the finder receives compensation without exceeding the slave's worth.
The deduction of one dirham is a symbolic measure to avoid the finder receiving the full value.
2
Understand the non-liability rule when the slave runs away again
If the recovered slave runs away from the finder without any transgression (ta'addi) by the finder, the finder is neither liable for the loss nor entitled to a reward. This is based on the precedent of Ali (r).
The opposite view of Shuraih, who held the finder liable, is rejected by the Hanafi school.
3
Note the recommended practice of taking witnesses
The text recommends (yandubu) that when taking the slave, the finder should have witnesses to prove he intends to return the slave to the owner, not to keep him.
This is a precautionary measure to avoid false accusations.
4
Identify who bears the reward when the slave is mortgaged
If the runaway slave is mortgaged (rahn), the reward for returning him falls upon the mortgagee (al-murtahin), because the mortgagee benefits from the slave's security.
The owner does not pay the reward in this case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيمته اقل من اربعين درهما قضى له بقيمته الا درهما وان ابق من الذي رده فلا شيء عليه ولا جُعل له وينبغي ان يشهد اذا اخذه انه يأخذ ليرد على صاحبه فان كان العبد الآبق رهنا فالجعل على المرتهن. وعن علي في الرجل يجد الآبق فيأبق منه لا يضمنه وضمنه شريح ونحن نقول بقول على ان كان الآبق ابق من دون تعديه
If his value is less than forty dirhams, he is awarded his value minus one dirham. And if he (the slave) runs away from the one who returned him, there is no liability upon him and no reward for him. And it is recommended that when he takes him, he should bear witness that he is taking him to return him to his owner. And if the runaway slave is mortgaged, the reward is upon the mortgagee. And from Ali (r) concerning a man who finds a runaway slave and he runs away from him: he is not liable, while Shuraih made him liable, and we follow the saying of Ali, provided that the slave ran away without his (the retriever's) transgression.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Ibaq presents several rulings. First, if the value of the runaway slave is less than forty dirhams, the finder is awarded the value minus one dirham. This is to ensure the finder gets some compensation while the owner does not pay more than the slave's value. Second, if the slave runs away from the person who found him (without any negligence), the finder is neither liable for the slave nor entitled to any reward. This is based on the effect of Ali (رضي الله عنه) that there is no liability, as opposed to Shuraih who held him liable. The Hanafi school follows Ali's view. Third, it is recommended (yandubu) to take witnesses when taking the slave, to avoid suspicion that the finder is taking him for himself. Fourth, if the slave is mortgaged (rahn), the reward for returning him falls upon the mortgagee (al-murtahin) because he is the one who benefits from the slave's security.

Key Terms
الآبق (al-ābiq): A runaway slave.
الجعل (al-juʿl): The reward or compensation given for returning the slave.
المرتهن (al-murtahin): The mortgagee, the person with whom the slave is mortgaged.
تعدي (taʿaddī): Transgression or negligence, an act beyond proper care.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how much reward does a finder receive for returning a runaway slave valued at 35 dirhams?
A) 35 dirhams
B) 34 dirhams
C) 40 dirhams
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school balances the finder's right to a reward with the owner's protection, and relieves the finder of liability for subsequent escape unless negligence occurs, based on the precedent of Ali (r).

Definition of Mawat Land and Conditions for Revival
Defines mawat land and gives prophetic proof for ownership via revival.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of mawat land, identify the three conditions that classify land as mawat, and explain how revival (ihya) establishes ownership based on the prophetic hadith.

Core Idea
Mawat land is dead, ownerless, far from habitation; reviving it gives you the right to own it.

Land that lies unused and unowned is a potential resource for the community. Islamic law encourages revival of such land while setting clear boundaries to prevent conflict. In this lesson, we examine the precise Hanafi definition of mawat land and the prophetic basis for ownership through revival.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitional elements
Note the three criteria: (1) land cannot be used due to water issues (lack or excess) or similar, (2) it has no known owner (ancient or unknown in Islam), (3) it is distant from habitation such that a shout from the last house is not heard.
All three must be present for land to be mawat.
2
Understand the legal implication
Revival (ihya) means making the land useful, e.g., by cultivation or building. The hadith states the reviver is 'more entitled' (ahaqq) to the land, which Hanafis interpret as full ownership.
The hadith is from Sahih al-Bukhari.
3
Apply to a scenario
Given a piece of land, check: is it beneficial? Is there a known owner? Is it far from the village? If all conditions met, it is mawat and revival leads to ownership (with permission of Imam, per Hanafi view).
If the land is near the village or has a known owner, it is not mawat and cannot be simply taken.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموات ما لا ينتفع به من الارض لانقطاع الماء عنه او لغلبة الماء عليه او ما اشبه ذلك مما يمنع الزراعة فما كان منها عاديا لا مالك له او كان مملوكا فى الاسلام لا يعرف له مالك بعينه وهو بعيد من القرية بحيث اذا وقف انسان في آخر العمران وصاح لم يسمع صوته فهي موات. عن عائشة عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال من اعمر ارضا ليست لاحد فهو احق
Al-mawat is land from which no benefit can be taken because of lack of water or because of excess water or similar that prevents cultivation. So whatever of it is ancient, having no owner, or was owned in Islam but no specific owner is known for it, and it is far from the village such that if a person stands at the last of the inhabited area and shouts, his voice is not heard, then it is mawat. From Aisha (r) from the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) that he said: 'Whoever brings life to land that does not belong to anyone, he is more entitled to it.'
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage from Kitab Ihya al-Mawat defines mawat land in three aspects. First, land that cannot be beneficially used due to lack of water (desert) or excess water (marshland) or any similar obstacle to cultivation. Second, land that has no known owner, either from ancient times (aadi) or where the owner is unknown (majhul). Third, land that is so distant from the nearest inhabited area that a person's shout from the last house cannot be heard. Such land can be revived (e.g., cultivated, built upon) with the permission of the Imam (ruler). The hadith from Aisha (r) establishes that the reviver has more right to the land. The Hanafi school holds that permission of the Imam is required for ownership, though some later Hanafis said it is not strictly necessary if the land is far from habitation.

Key Terms
الموات (al-mawāt): Dead land that is not cultivable or usable due to natural impediments and without an owner.
الإحياء (al-iḥyāʾ): The act of reviving dead land by cultivation, building, or any means that makes it useful.
عاديا (ʿādiyan): Ancient land that has never been owned or where the owner is lost in antiquity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition for land to be considered mawat?
A) It cannot be cultivated due to lack of water
B) It has a known owner who is absent
C) It is so far from the village that a shout from the last house cannot be heard
D) It is ancient land with no known owner

Key Takeaway
Mawat land has three defining conditions: 1) unbeneficial due to water hindrance, 2) no known owner, 3) distant from habitation; reviving it grants ownership based on the prophetic 'ahaqq' (more entitled).

Definition of Mawat and Ownership by Revival with Imam's Permission
Defining dead land and revival condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of mawat (dead land) and identify the condition required for ownership through revival.

Core Idea
Dead land is defined by distance from habitation, not just lack of cultivation; ownership requires imam's permission.

This passage from the matn gives the foundational Hanafi rule on dead land (mawat). It defines mawat by a clear physical criterion—distance from inhabited areas—and establishes that revival (ihya) only grants ownership if done with the imam's permission. This rule is a memorization anchor for the conditions of acquiring mawat land.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of mawat
The phrase 'في اقصى العامر فصاح لم يسمع الصوت فيه' gives the defining criterion: distance from habitation measured by audibility of a human call.
2
Identify the ruling on revival
The text states 'من احياه باذن الامام ملکه' — whoever revives it with the imam's permission owns it. Note the conditional 'with permission'.
3
Reflect on the thamarah al-khilaf
If revival without permission, Hanafi says ownership does not transfer; Shafi'i disagrees. This difference matters in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في اقصى العامر فصاح لم يسمع الصوت فيه فهو موات من احياه باذن الامام ملکه
In the furthest part of the inhabited land where the voice of the caller is not heard, it is dead land. Whoever revives it with the permission of the imam owns it.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn defining mawat (dead land) and the prerequisite for ownership. Dead land is defined by distance: if a person at the edge of the inhabited area calls out and no one hears, that land is mawat. The revival must be with the imam's permission to grant ownership. This is the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Key Terms
موات (mawāt): Land that is dead, uncultivated, and at a distance from inhabited areas where human voice is not heard.
احیاء (iḥyā’): Reviving dead land by cultivation, building, or other means to make it productive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The land must be owned by the state.
D) The land must be arid and barren.

Key Takeaway
Dead land (mawat) is defined by distance from habitation, and ownership through revival requires the imam's permission.

Revival Without Imam's Permission: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on ownership via iḥyā’ without imam's permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on ownership of revived dead land without the imam's permission and explain the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Revival of dead land: Abu Hanifa denies ownership without permission; Sahibayn grant it—a key intra-Hanafi split.

The revival of dead land (iḥyā’ al-mawāt) is a well-known means of acquiring ownership in Islamic law. However, a significant intra-Hanafi dispute exists regarding whether the reviver must obtain the imam's permission to own the land. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that permission is required, while his two leading students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), argue that permission is not necessary. Understanding this ikhtilaf and its practical consequences is essential for applying the ruling correctly in different contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Dispute
What is iḥyā’? What is the contested condition for ownership?
Iḥyā’ means making dead land productive, e.g., by farming or building.
2
State Each Position with Its Proof
State Abu Hanifa's position (no ownership without permission) and the Sahibayn's position (ownership established upon revival). Cite their respective evidences: Abu Hanifa's dalil (land is fay’ requiring imam's distribution) and the Sahibayn's dalil (the hadith 'Whoever revives dead land, it belongs to him').
The hadith is narrated by Sa'id ibn Zayd and is considered ṣaḥīḥ.
3
Analyse the Hanafi Response
How does Abu Hanifa counter the hadith evidence? He argues that the general command of the hadith is specified (takhṣīṣ) by the need for the imam's permission to prevent chaos and conflicting claims.
This is a case of takhṣīṣ al-‘āmm based on another textual evidence or maṣlaḥah.
4
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
What is the practical difference in ownership depending on the existence of a ruler? In a structured state, Abu Hanifa's view prevents arbitrary land claims. In the absence of a ruler, the Sahibayn's view encourages land reclamation.
The muftā bihī in the Hanafi school for fatwa in a structured state is Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان احیاه بغیر اذنه لم يملكه عند ابي حنيفة رحمه الله وقالا رحمهما الله يملكه
And if he revives it without his permission, he does not own it according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said, may Allah have mercy on them both, he owns it.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. Abu Hanifa holds that revival of dead land without the imam's permission does not confer ownership; the reviver must seek permission. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that ownership is established even without permission, citing a hadith 'Whoever revives dead land, it belongs to him.' The mufta bihi position in later Hanafi works is the view of Abu Hanifa due to the need for public order.

Key Terms
احیاء (iḥyā’): Revival of dead land by making it productive, e.g., cultivating or building.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the position of Abu Hanifa regarding reviving dead land without the imam's permission?
A) The reviver becomes owner.
B) The reviver does not become owner.
C) Ownership depends on the type of revival.
D) Ownership is half.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa requires the imam's permission for ownership of revived dead land, while the Sahibayn do not; the mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view for fatwa in a structured state.

Dhimmi Ownership by Revival
Ruling statement on dhimmi property rights through revival

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a dhimmi owns revived dead land like a Muslim and identify the condition of imam's permission according to Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
Dhimmis own revived land equally – no discrimination in mubahat ownership.

This short but powerful statement establishes equality between Muslims and dhimmis in acquiring dead land through revival. It is a foundational rule in Hanafi property law, reflecting the principle that dhimmis, after paying jizyah, enjoy the same rights as Muslims in common properties (mubahat).

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and act
The subject is the dhimmi (non-Muslim citizen) and the act is revival (ihya) of dead land.
The act is a cause of ownership in Hanafi law.
2
Note the comparison
The ruling is explicitly compared to the Muslim's ownership: 'just as the Muslim owns it.'
This implies identical legal effect.
3
Understand the implied condition (optional)
Per Abu Hanifa, revival requires the imam's permission for both Muslims and dhimmis alike. The text does not mention this but the school's detail is important.
Muhammad al-Shaybani held permission is not required.
4
Apply to a scenario
A dhimmi revives a barren plot. He becomes owner and can sell, gift, or inherit it.
No extra tax or restriction beyond those for Muslims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويملك الذمى بالاحياء كما يملكه المسلم
And the dhimmi owns by revival just as the Muslim owns it.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This is a ruling statement affirming that a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state) has the same right as a Muslim to own dead land through revival, provided they obtain the imam's permission (per Abu Hanifa). This is based on the principle that dhimmis, upon paying jizyah, have equal rights in property acquisition.

Key Terms
ذمی (dhimmī): A non-Muslim living under the protection of the Islamic state, paying jizyah.
إحياء (iḥyāʾ): Revival of dead land through cultivation or construction, a cause of ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Muslims and dhimmis equally
C) Only dhimmis
D) Only the imam

Key Takeaway
A dhimmi owns revived dead land exactly as a Muslim does, reflecting the Hanafi principle of equal property rights for dhimmis.

Tahjir and the Three-Year Limit for Revival
Ruling on marking land and the consequence of not reviving within three years

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the consequence of failing to revive marked dead land within three years and explain the Hanafi rationale for this limit.

Core Idea
Marking land gives priority, but failure to revive within three years forfeits that right to the imam.

Tahjir is the first step in claiming dead land. However, it is not ownership; it is a priority right that must be followed by actual revival. This lesson examines the limit set by the Hanafi school: if the land is not revived within three years, the imam can reassign it to someone else. This ensures that land is productive and not hoarded.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act: tahjir
Tahjir is marking the boundaries of dead land with stones or similar markers to indicate a claim. It is a permissible act that gives a priority right to revive the land.
2
Understand the condition: revival within three years
The marker must actually revive the land (e.g., by cultivating, building, or otherwise making it productive) within three years. This is not a pillar (rukn) of tahjir but a condition for retaining the priority right.
The three-year limit is based on the practice of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab (RA).
3
Recognize the consequence: forfeiture and reassignment
If the marker fails to revive the land within three years, the imam (state authority) takes the land from him and grants it to another person who will revive it. The original marker loses all rights.
4
Note the authority and wisdom
This ruling is based on the precedent of 'Umar (RA) with Bilaal ibn al-Haarith. It prevents hoarding and encourages productive use of land, which is a public interest (maslahah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن حجر ارضا ولم يعمرها ثلث سنين اخذها الامام منه ودفعها الى غیره
And whoever marks a land and does not revive it for three years, the imam takes it from him and gives it to another.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This ruling addresses tahjir (marking boundaries with stones) as a preliminary claim. If a person marks land but fails to actually revive it within three years, his right is forfeited and the imam can reassign the land to someone else who will revive it. This is based on the practice of 'Umar (RA) with Bilaal ibn al-Haarith.

Key Terms
تحجیر (taḥjīr): Marking the boundaries of dead land with stones to indicate a claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The marker is fined.
B) The imam takes the land and gives it to another.
C) The marker can extend the period by renewing the markers.
D) The land remains unclaimed.

Key Takeaway
Tahjir alone does not grant ownership; the marker must revive the land within three years or the imam may reassign it.

Prohibition of Reviving Land Near Inhabited Area
Ruling statement prohibiting revival of land near habitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on reviving land near inhabited areas and identify the communal rights reserved over such land.

Core Idea
Land near a village cannot be privately owned; it is reserved for communal grazing and village needs.

The revival (ihya) of dead land (mawat) is generally permitted in Hanafi fiqh as a means of bringing barren land into productive use. However, this right is restricted when the land is adjacent to an inhabited area (amir). This lesson explains that such land must be left as a common pasture (mar'a) for the villagers, ensuring communal benefit and preventing hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is reviving dead land (ihya al-mawat) that is near an inhabited area. The ruling: it is not permissible (la yajuz) – meaning prohibited (haram) in the Hanafi school.
This is a clear prohibition; the land cannot become private property.
2
Locate the condition
The condition triggering the prohibition is proximity to the ‘amir’ (inhabited/cultivated area). If the land is far from habitation, the ruling may differ.
‘Amir refers to the village or town boundary, including its immediate vicinity.
3
Identify the consequence
Such land is left as a common pasture (mar‘ā) for the people of the village, along with other communal uses (threshing floors, wood collection, etc.).
The land remains in public ownership; no individual can claim it.
4
Note the hikmah (legal reasoning)
The prohibition prevents hardship to villagers and ensures access to essential resources (grazing, gathering). It safeguards communal welfare over private enrichment.
This is based on the principle of darar (harm) being removed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يجوز احياء مـا قـرب مـن الـعامر ويترك مرعى لاهل القرية
And it is not permissible to revive what is near the inhabited area, and it is left as pasture for the people of the village.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This ruling states that dead land immediately adjacent to inhabited areas cannot be revived for private ownership. Such land is reserved as communal pasture (mar'a) and for other needs of the villagers, such as threshing floors or wood collection. This is to prevent hardship and ensure communal access.

Key Terms
عامر (‘āmir): Inhabited or cultivated area; land that is actively used, including the village and its immediate surroundings.
مرعى (mar‘ā): Pasture land for grazing animals; communal land reserved for the villagers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to land that is near an inhabited area?
A) It can be revived with the imam's permission.
B) It is left as a common pasture for the villagers.
C) It becomes the property of the imam.
D) It is taxed and then can be revived.

Key Takeaway
Land near an inhabited area cannot be revived for private ownership; it must remain communal pasture for the villagers.

Tahjir and Proximity to Settlements in Ihya al-Mawat
Presents ruling on tahjir and proximity to villages

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tahjir (three-year limit) and explain why land near villages cannot be revived for private ownership.

Core Idea
Marking dead land does not confer permanent ownership; revival must occur within three years, and public land near settlements is preserved for common benefit.

Reviving dead land (ihya al-mawat) is a means of ownership in the Hanafi school, but it comes with conditions to protect public interest. This lesson examines two key regulations: the time limit on a mere claim (tahjir) and the prohibition of reviving land too close to villages, which is reserved for communal use. These rules balance private initiative with the welfare of the community.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
First, recognise that tahjir (placing stones to mark a claim) is not an act of revival. The ruling: if the land is not cultivated within three years, the imam may take it and give to another. Also, land near inhabited areas cannot be revived at all; it remains common property.
Tahjir is a mere indicator of intent, not ownership.
2
State the condition and consequence
For tahjir, the condition is that revival (actual cultivation or construction) must occur within three years. If not, the right is lost. For proximity land, the condition is that the land must be within a certain distance (e.g., a bowshot) from the village; such land is excluded from private revival.
The three-year limit is based on 'Umar's practice.
3
Locate the dalil (evidence)
The evidence for the three-year rule is the hadith of Bilal ibn al-Harith and 'Umar's statement: 'The one who marks has no right after three years.' For the proximity rule, the hadith of Jabir: 'Whoever revives a land at a bowshot from the town, it is his.' The commentary deduces that land closer than that is reserved for public use.
Both evidences are sunnah ahad, zanni in thubut.
4
Analyse the wisdom (hikmah)
Tahjir without cultivation would allow hoarding of land. Three years is considered a reasonable period to test intent and ability. Prohibiting revival near towns preserves space for grazing, drying crops, and other communal needs.
The principle of 'la darar wa la dirar' underlies the rules.
5
Apply to a scenario
A man marks a plot of dead land with stones but does nothing else. After four years, can the imam reassign it? Yes, because the three-year limit has passed. Another person wants to revive a field adjacent to the village boundary, which is within 300 cubits. Can he? No, that land is reserved for village common use.
The exact distance for proximity is defined by 'ramya' (a bowshot) or 'ghulwa' (about 300-400 cubits).

Chapter on Reviving Dead Lands. (5) Whoever marks a land and does not cultivate it for three years, the imam takes it from him and gives it to another. (6) It is not permissible to revive land near inhabited areas; it is left as pasture for the villagers and a place for their harvested crops. Commentary: In Islamic law, after paying jizya, the rights of a dhimmi are like those of a Muslim. There is indication in the following: Ibn 'Abbas said: 'When they pay jizya, their wealth is not permissible for you except with their consent.' (5) Someone placed a stone marker on land and did not cultivate it for three years; the imam takes it and gives to another. The Prophet gave dead land to Bilal ibn al-Harith. He did not cultivate it for several years; the uncultivated part was taken and given to another. From al-Harith ibn Bilal from his father: The Messenger of Allah took the minerals of the tribe as sadaqa and granted Bilal the whole of al-'Aqiq. When 'Umar came, he said to Bilal: The Messenger did not grant it to you to keep from people, but to work it. So 'Umar gave al-'Aqiq to the people. (al-Bayhaqi) From this it is understood that if one merely marks and does not cultivate, it is taken. The condition of three years is in the effect of 'Umar: 'Whoever revives a dead land, it is his, and the one who marks has no right after three years.' (al-Rayah) So after three years he has no right. This land is given for cultivation, not marking; after three years it is taken back and distributed. (6) It is not permissible to revive land near dwellings; it is left as pasture for villagers and for their harvested crops. The empty land near villages should not be given to anyone; it is for the benefit of the villagers: for grazing animals and for placing harvested crops. Footnotes: (a) The Prophet took the minerals as sadaqa and gave al-'Aqiq to Bilal; 'Umar told Bilal that the grant was for work, not enclosure, so 'Umar gave it to the people. (b) 'Umar said: Whoever revives dead land, it is his; the marker has no right after three years. Then the text continues: ... (7) Whoever digs a well in the wilderness has its harim. The protected area around a well prevents others from digging close. (8) If the well is for watering livestock, its harim is 40 cubits; if for irrigation, 60 cubits; if it is a spring, 500 cubits. Evidence: hadith from Jabir about distance from town, and hadith from 'Abdullah ibn Mughaffal: 'Whoever digs a well has 40 cubits as a resting place for his animals.' (Ibn Majah) Footnotes: (a) The Prophet said whoever revives land at a bowshot from the town, it is his. (b) The Prophet said: Whoever digs a well has 40 cubits as harim for livestock.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage from al-Sharh al-Thamiri covers fundamental rulings on the revival of dead lands (ihya' al-mawat). The first ruling establishes that marking land (tahjir) does not confer permanent ownership; the imam may reassign the land after three years of neglect, supported by the practice of 'Umar and the story of Bilal ibn al-Harith. The second ruling prohibits the revival of land too close to villages, preserving it for common use as pasture and for harvested crops. The third and fourth rulings define the concept of harim (protected area) for wells and springs, with varying distances depending on the purpose: 40 cubits for livestock, 60 for irrigation, and 500 for springs. The harim prevents harm to existing water sources by prohibiting new wells within these distances. The evidence includes hadith from Jabir (about distance from town) and from 'Abdullah ibn Mughaffal (about harim of 40 cubits). The commentary emphasizes public interest and the principle of no harm. The author also notes that dhimmis have equal rights to dead land after paying jizya. The footnotes provide the sources of the hadith. The overall lesson is that while reviving dead land is encouraged, it is subject to regulations to balance private benefit and public welfare.

Key Terms
تحجير (tahjir): Marking a plot of dead land with stones to indicate a claim pending revival.
احياء الموات (ihya' al-mawat): Bringing dead, uncultivated land into productive use, such as by farming or construction.
العامر (al-'amir): Inhabited area, village or town.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The marker becomes the owner automatically after three years.
B) The imam takes the land and gives it to someone else.
C) The marker must pay a penalty but keeps the land.
D) The land remains unowned forever.

Key Takeaway
Mere marking of dead land does not confer ownership; revival must occur within three years, and land near settlements is reserved for public use.

Harim of Wells and Springs: Protected Areas in Ihya al-Mawat
Presents rulings on harim of wells with specific distances

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the harim distances for different types of wells and apply the rule to prevent harm to existing water sources.

Core Idea
The protected area around a well varies by purpose: 40 cubits for livestock, 60 for irrigation, 500 for springs, all to prevent injury to the original water source.

When a person digs a well in the wilderness, they acquire not only the well but also a protective zone (harim) around it. This prevents others from digging competing wells that could damage the water supply. The Hanafi school specifies different distances based on the well's use, as established by hadith and the principle of no harm.

Guided Path
1
Define harim and its purpose
Harim is the area around a well or spring where digging another well is forbidden to prevent the first from drying up or being polluted. The purpose is to avoid harm (la darar).
Harim applies to wells dug in unowned land (wilderness).
2
Identify the three categories of wells
The passage lists three: well for watering livestock ('atn), well for irrigation (nadih), and a spring ('ayn). Each has a different harim distance.
The term 'atn refers to a resting place for animals, i.e., a livestock well.
3
Memorize the distances
For livestock well: 40 cubits; for irrigation well: 60 cubits; for spring: 500 cubits. These are from the hadith of 'Abdullah ibn Mughaffal and the practice of the Companions.
One cubit is approximately the length from elbow to fingertips (roughly 45-50 cm).
4
Connect the evidence
The primary evidence is the hadith: 'Whoever digs a well has 40 cubits as a resting place for his animals.' (Ibn Majah). The larger distances for irrigation and springs are based on the greater need for water and the wider spread of water underground.
The hadith is sunnah ahad, zanni in transmission but accepted in practice.
5
Apply to a scenario
A farmer digs a well for irrigating his fields. Can a neighbour dig a well 50 cubits away? No, because the harim for irrigation is 60 cubits—the neighbour would be within the protected area and can be prevented.
The rule is enforced by the imam or local authority.

Chapter on Reviving Dead Lands. (5) Whoever marks a land and does not cultivate it for three years, the imam takes it from him and gives it to another. (6) It is not permissible to revive land near inhabited areas; it is left as pasture for the villagers and a place for their harvested crops. Commentary: In Islamic law, after paying jizya, the rights of a dhimmi are like those of a Muslim. There is indication in the following: Ibn 'Abbas said: 'When they pay jizya, their wealth is not permissible for you except with their consent.' (5) Someone placed a stone marker on land and did not cultivate it for three years; the imam takes it and gives to another. The Prophet gave dead land to Bilal ibn al-Harith. He did not cultivate it for several years; the uncultivated part was taken and given to another. From al-Harith ibn Bilal from his father: The Messenger of Allah took the minerals of the tribe as sadaqa and granted Bilal the whole of al-'Aqiq. When 'Umar came, he said to Bilal: The Messenger did not grant it to you to keep from people, but to work it. So 'Umar gave al-'Aqiq to the people. (al-Bayhaqi) From this it is understood that if one merely marks and does not cultivate, it is taken. The condition of three years is in the effect of 'Umar: 'Whoever revives a dead land, it is his, and the one who marks has no right after three years.' (al-Rayah) So after three years he has no right. This land is given for cultivation, not marking; after three years it is taken back and distributed. (6) It is not permissible to revive land near dwellings; it is left as pasture for villagers and for their harvested crops. The empty land near villages should not be given to anyone; it is for the benefit of the villagers: for grazing animals and for placing harvested crops. Footnotes: (a) The Prophet took the minerals as sadaqa and gave al-'Aqiq to Bilal; 'Umar told Bilal that the grant was for work, not enclosure, so 'Umar gave it to the people. (b) 'Umar said: Whoever revives dead land, it is his; the marker has no right after three years. Then the text continues: ... (7) Whoever digs a well in the wilderness has its harim. The protected area around a well prevents others from digging close. (8) If the well is for watering livestock, its harim is 40 cubits; if for irrigation, 60 cubits; if it is a spring, 500 cubits. Evidence: hadith from Jabir about distance from town, and hadith from 'Abdullah ibn Mughaffal: 'Whoever digs a well has 40 cubits as a resting place for his animals.' (Ibn Majah) Footnotes: (a) The Prophet said whoever revives land at a bowshot from the town, it is his. (b) The Prophet said: Whoever digs a well has 40 cubits as harim for livestock.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage from al-Sharh al-Thamiri covers fundamental rulings on the revival of dead lands (ihya' al-mawat). The first ruling establishes that marking land (tahjir) does not confer permanent ownership; the imam may reassign the land after three years of neglect, supported by the practice of 'Umar and the story of Bilal ibn al-Harith. The second ruling prohibits the revival of land too close to villages, preserving it for common use as pasture and for harvested crops. The third and fourth rulings define the concept of harim (protected area) for wells and springs, with varying distances depending on the purpose: 40 cubits for livestock, 60 for irrigation, and 500 for springs. The harim prevents harm to existing water sources by prohibiting new wells within these distances. The evidence includes hadith from Jabir (about distance from town) and from 'Abdullah ibn Mughaffal (about harim of 40 cubits). The commentary emphasizes public interest and the principle of no harm. The author also notes that dhimmis have equal rights to dead land after paying jizya. The footnotes provide the sources of the hadith. The overall lesson is that while reviving dead land is encouraged, it is subject to regulations to balance private benefit and public welfare.

Key Terms
حریم (harim): The protected area around a well or spring where digging another well is prohibited to prevent harm to the original water source.
عطن ('atn): A resting place for livestock near a well, used for watering.
ناضح (nadih): A well used for irrigation (literally, a well from which water is drawn for crops).

intermediate

Harim of Wells and Springs and Riverbed Revival
Commentary with hadith proofs on harim rulings and riverbed revival.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the harim measurements for different types of wells and springs according to the Hanafi school, identify the hadith proofs, and apply the ruling for reviving an abandoned riverbed.

Core Idea
Harim size depends on use: 40 cubits for livestock, 60 for irrigation, 500 for a spring.

This passage from Al-Sharh al-Thamiri explains the Hanafi rulings on the harim (protected buffer zone) around wells and springs. It provides specific measurements based on the well's purpose and supports them with authentic hadith. The second part covers the legal status of riverbeds that have been abandoned by their water—whether they can be revived as dead land or must remain public utility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? Digging a well in dead land is mubah, but establishing a harim is a protective right to prevent harm.
The principle of la darar wa la dirar underlies the harim.
2
Determine the harim measurements for each well type
Three categories: 'atn (livestock) → 40 cubits; nadih (irrigation) → 60 cubits; spring ('ayn) → 500 cubits. The harim extends on all sides.
The text also mentions alternative narrations (e.g., 50 cubits for desert well) but settles on these.
3
Locate the dalil
Two key hadith: (1) Ibn Mughaffal: 40 cubits for livestock well; (2) Abu Hurairah: measurements for village well, desert well, flowing spring, and irrigation spring.
These are ahad hadith, sufficient for establishing rulings in Hanafi usul.
4
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's explicit statements directly prescribe the numbers. In Hanafi usul, such explicit commands (amr) indicate obligation of respect for the harim, though the harim itself is a right, not a fard obligation.
The hadith of Abu Hurairah also adds 'min nawahiha kulliha' indicating all sides.
5
Apply the rule to abandoned riverbeds
If water can return to its original channel, the land cannot be revived because it remains public utility. If return is impossible, it becomes dead land eligible for reviving with the ruler's permission.
This balances private ownership with public interest in waterways.

و اس سے قریب میں بھی زمین آباد کرے گا اور امام اجازت دیدے تو مالک ہو جائے گا۔
اصول بہتر یہ ہے کہ رفاہ عام کی جگہ کو کسی کی ملکیت قرار نہ دے۔
ان مرعی : چرنے کی جگہ ، رمی سے مشتق ہے، حصائد : کٹی ہوئی کھیتی، العامر : آبادی۔ [1668] (7) کسی نے جنگل میں کنواں کھودا تو اس کے لئے اس کا حریم ہے۔ کنواں کے چاروں طرف جو جگہ چھوڑ دیتے ہیں تا کہ اس میں کوئی دوسرا کنواں نہ کھو دے اس کو کنواں کا حریم کہتے ہیں۔ یہ اس لئے ہوتا ہے تا کہ پہلے کنویں کے قریب کوئی کنواں کھودے تو اس کا پانی دوسرے میں نہ چلا جائے اور پہلا کنواں سوکھ نہ جائے ۔ یا دوسرے کنویں کی گندگی پہلے کنواں میں نہ پہنچ جائے۔ اس لئے اس کے قریب بغیر اجازت کے دوسرا کنواں کھودنے نہیں دیا جائے گا۔
اصول یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ دوسرے کو نقصان نہ پہنچے ، لا ضرر ولا ضرار.

[19] (8) پس اگر وہ کنواں پانی پلانے کے لئے ہو تو اس کا حریم چالیس ہاتھ ہے۔ اور اگر کھیت سیراب کرنے کے لئے ہو تو اس کا حریم ساتھ ہاتھ ہے۔ اور اگر چشمہ ہو تو اس کا حریم پانچ سو ہاتھ ہے۔ پس اگر کوئی اس کے حریم میں کنواں کھودنا چاہے تو اس سے روکا جائے گا۔ ال اگر کنواں اونٹ کو پانی پلانے کے لئے ہے تو اس کا حریم چالیس هاتھ ہے۔ اور اگر کھیتوں کو سیراب کرنے کے لئے ہے تو اس کا حریم ساتھ هاتھ ہے۔ اور اگر چشمہ ہے تو اس کا حریم پانچ سو هاتھ ہے۔ کیونکہ چشمہ کا پانی پھلتا ہے۔
اس حدیث میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ عن عبد الله بن مغفل ان النبي الله قال من حفر بئرا فله اربعون ذراعا عطنا لماشيته (ب) (ابن ماجہ شریف، باب حریم البئر ص ۳۵۶ نمبر ۲۳۸۶) اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ اونٹ کو پانی پلانے والے کنویں کے لئے حریم حاشیہ : (الف) آپ سے مروی ہے کسی نے تیر پھینکنے کے مطابق کی دوری پر زمین آباد کی تو وہ زمین اسی کی ہے (ب) آپ نے فرمایا کسی نے کنواں کھودا تو اس کے لئے چالیس ہاتھ حریم ہے جانور کو پانی پلانے کے لئے.
وما ترك الفرات او الدجلة وعدل عنه الماء فان كان يجوز عوده اليه لم يجز احياؤها وان كان لا يمكن ان يعود اليه فهو كالموات اذا لم يكن حريما (الشرح الثميرى الجزء الثانى) 429 كتاب احياء الموات
عينا فحريمها خمس مائة ذراع فمن اراد ان يحفر بئرا في حريمها منع منه [ ١٧٠ ](۹) وما ترك الفرات او الدجلة وعدل عنه الماء فان كان يجوز عوده اليه لم يجز احياؤها وان كان لا يمكن ان يعود اليه فهو كالموات اذا لم يكن حريما چالیس هاتھ ہو گا۔ اور یہ چاروں طرف چالیس چالیس هاتھ ہوگا۔ اور کھیتی سیراب کرنے والے کنویں کے لئے پچاس پاتھ حریم ہو اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن ابي هريرة قال قال رسول الله الا الله حريم البئر البدى خمسة و عشرون ذراعا وحريم البئر العادية خمسون ذراعا وحريم العين السائحة ثلاث مائة ذراع وحريم عين الزرع ست مائة ذراع (الف) (دار قطني ، كتاب في : الاقضية والا حکام و غیر ذلک ، ج رابع ص ۱۴۱ نمبر ۴۴۷۳ رسنن بیہقی ، باب ما جاء فی حریم الآبار، ج سادس ہیں ۲۵۷، نمبر ۱۱۸۶۹) اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ پار عادیہ یعنی کھیتی میں پانی پلانے والے کنویں کے لئے حریم پچاس هاتھ ہو گا۔ اور بیہقی کے اثر میں یہ بھی اضافہ ہے نواحيها كلها جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ کنویں کے چاروں طرف پچاس پچاس هاتھ حریم ہونا چاہئے (۲) یوں بھی کھیتی سیراب کرنے کے لئے اونٹ چاروں طرف گھومتا ہے۔ اس لئے چاروں طرف پچاس پچاس هاتھ حریم چاہئے اور چشمے کا حریم پانچ سو هاتھ ہو اس کی ایک دلیل اوپر کی حدیث گزری حریم عين الزرع ست مائة ذراع اور دوسرا اثر یہ ہے وقال الزهرى وسمعت الناس يقولون حريم العيون خمسمائة ذراع (ب) (سن للمبتی ، باب ما جاء فی حریم الآبار، ج سادس ص ۲۵۷ نمبر ۱۱۸۶۹) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ چشمے کے لئے
پانچ سو هاتھ حریم ہونا چاہئے۔ انت عطن : اونٹ کو پانی پلانے کا کنواں، ناضح : کھیتی سیراب کرنے کا کنواں، یا وہ اونٹ جس سے کھیتی سیراب کی جاتی ہے۔ [1670] (9) جوز مين فرات اور دجلہ نہر نے چھوڑ دی اور پانی اس سے ہٹ گیا۔ پس اگر اس کا اس طرف لوٹنا ممکن ہو تو اس کا آباد کرنا جائز
نہیں ہے۔ ر فرات یاد جلہ ندی مثلا ایک جگہ سے بہ رہی تھی۔ اور وہاں چھوڑ کر دوسری جگہ بہنا شروع کر دیا تو اندازہ لگائے کہ دوبارہ اپنی جگہ پر آنے کا انداز ہے یا نہیں۔ اگر دوبارہ اپنی جگہ پر آنے کا انداز ہے تو اس جگہ کو آباد کرنے کے لئے دینا جائز نہیں ہے۔ نہر بہنے کے لئے چاہئے ورنہ اتنا پانی کس راستے سے جائے گا۔ اس لئے یہ عوام کے فائدے کی جگہ ہے اس لئے اس کو آباد کرنے نہ دی جائے۔ اور اگر دوبارہ اس جگہ پر آنے کا امکان نہ ہو تو وہ زمین موات کی طرح ہے۔ اگر کسی کا اس کے ساتھ حق متعلق نہ ہو اور امام کی اجازت سے اس کو آباد کرے تو وہ اس کا مالک ہو جائے گا۔ [1971] (10) اور اگر نہیں ممکن ہے کہ اس کی طرف لوٹے تو وہ موات کی زمین کی طرح ہے۔ اگر کسی آباد کرنے والے کا حریم نہ ہو تو اس کا مالک حاشیہ : (الف) آپ نے فرمایا دیہاتی کنویں کا حریم پچیس هاتھ اور جنگل کے کنویں کا حریم پچاس هاتھ ، زمین پر بہنے والے چشمے کا حریم تین سو هاتھ اور کھیتی کے چشمے کا حریم چھ سو هاتھ ہے ( ب ) حضرت زہری نے فرمایا کہ لوگوں کو کہتے ہوئے سنا ہے چشمے کا حریم پانچ سو هاتھ ہونا چاہئے.
Book of Reviving Dead Lands. A place for their harvests. Whoever digs a well in the wilderness, he has its harim. If it is for watering animals, its harim is forty cubits; if it is for irrigation, its harim is sixty cubits; and if it is for a spring, its harim is five hundred cubits. (2) There is a hadith: Jabir ibn Abdullah narrated that the Prophet said: Whoever revives dead land at a distance of a bowshot or an arrow shot from a village, it is his. (Musnad Ahmad, no. 14496). This hadith indicates that one may revive dead land at a bowshot or arrow's flight from a village. I heard Ikrimah say: The Messenger of Allah said: Allah has made for cultivation a sanctity of a bowshot. Yahya said: They said: The bowshot is between three hundred fifty and four hundred cubits. (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, no. 11873). In this hadith, he forbade cultivation up to a bowshot (i.e., 350 cubits) so that the villagers can use that land for public welfare. If someone cultivates closer than that with the ruler's permission, he becomes owner. The best principle is not to assign ownership to public welfare land. Al-mur'a: grazing place, derived from rami; hasa'id: reaped crops; al-'amir: inhabited. (7) If someone digs a well in the desert, he has its harim. The harim of a well is the area left around it so that no one else digs another well there, to prevent the water of the first from flowing to the second and causing it to dry up, or to prevent contamination. So no one may dig another well nearby without permission. This issue is based on the principle: do not cause harm, no harm and no reciprocating harm. (8) If the well is for watering animals, its harim is forty cubits; if for irrigating fields, its harim is sixty cubits; if it is a spring, its harim is five hundred cubits. So if someone tries to dig within its harim, he is prevented. The proof is the hadith of Abdullah ibn Mughaffal that the Prophet said: Whoever digs a well, he has forty cubits as a resting place for his livestock. (Ibn Majah, no. 2386). From this it is understood that for a well for watering camels, the harim is forty cubits. Footnote: (a) It is narrated from him that whoever revives land at the distance of an arrow shot, that land is his. (b) The Prophet said: Whoever digs a well, he has forty cubits as harim for watering animals. And regarding what the Euphrates or Tigris leaves and the water turns away from it, if its return is possible, its revival is not allowed; but if it is impossible for it to return, it is like dead land if it is not a harim. (Commentary continues on page 434). The harim of a spring is five hundred cubits, and whoever intends to dig a well in its harim is prevented from it. (9) And what the Euphrates or Tigris leaves and the water turns away from it, if its return is possible, its revival is not allowed; but if it is impossible for it to return, it is like dead land if it is not a harim. It will be forty cubits, and this is forty cubits on all sides. For an irrigation well, the harim is fifty cubits; its proof is the hadith of Abu Hurairah that the Prophet said: The harim of a village well is twenty-five cubits, and of a desert well is fifty cubits, and of a flowing spring is three hundred cubits, and of a spring for irrigation is six hundred cubits. (Dar Qutni, no. 4473; al-Bayhaqi, no. 11869). From this it is understood that for a well used for irrigation ('adiyyah), the harim is fifty cubits. In the report of al-Bayhaqi, there is an addition: 'from all its sides', indicating that the harim extends fifty cubits on each side of the well. Similarly, because the camel used for irrigation moves around all sides, the harim must be fifty cubits on all sides. For a spring, the harim is five hundred cubits; one proof is the above hadith mentioning the harim of an irrigation spring as six hundred cubits, and another report is that al-Zuhri said: I heard people say the harim of springs is five hundred cubits. (al-Bayhaqi, no. 11869). From this it is known that for a spring, the harim is five hundred cubits. Glossary: 'atn: a well for watering camels; nadih: a well for irrigation, or the camel used for irrigation. (9) For example, if the Euphrates or Tigris river changes course and water leaves its bed, then if it is likely to return, its revival is not permissible. One must assess whether it can return to its original place. If it can return, it cannot be given for revival, because the river needs a channel; otherwise, the water would have no path. This area is for public benefit, so it should not be allowed to be cultivated. But if it is unlikely that the water will return, then that land is like dead land. If no one else has a right attached to it, and the ruler gives permission to revive it, then the reviver becomes its owner. (10) And if it is impossible for it to return, it is like dead land. If the reviver has no harim, he becomes its owner. Footnote: (a) The Prophet said: The harim of a village well is twenty-five cubits, of a desert well is fifty cubits, of a flowing spring is three hundred cubits, and of a spring for irrigation is six hundred cubits. (b) Al-Zuhri said: People say the harim of springs is five hundred cubits.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage from Al-Sharh al-Thamiri explains the rulings on harim (protected area) for wells and springs in the context of reviving dead land (ihya al-mawat). The passage begins by quoting the base ruling: whoever digs a well in the wilderness has a harim around it. The harim is the buffer zone left to prevent harm to the well's water supply or quality. The ruling is based on the principle of la darar wa la dirar (no harm, no reciprocating harm). The size of the harim depends on the well's purpose: if it is a 'atn (for watering camels and livestock), the harim is forty cubits on all sides; if it is a nadih (for irrigation), it is sixty cubits (though some narrations mention fifty cubits for a desert well and sixty for the actual well? The text reconciles by noting different classifications). For a spring (ayn), the harim is five hundred cubits because its water spreads more. The author provides hadith proofs from Ibn Majah and other sources, including a hadith from Abdullah ibn Mughaffal specifying forty cubits for a well for livestock, and a hadith from Abu Hurairah detailing different harims for village wells, desert wells, flowing springs, and irrigation springs. The commentary emphasizes that the harim extends in all directions (nawahiha kulluha), not just one side. The latter part of the passage addresses riverbeds: if a river like the Euphrates or Tigris abandons its original channel and the water may return, that land cannot be revived because it remains public utility. But if the water cannot return, the land becomes like dead land and can be revived with the ruler's permission, subject to no other rights. This aligns with the Hanafi position that public waterways are not to be privatized.

Key Terms
حریم (harim): The buffer zone around a well or spring that is protected from others digging or building nearby to prevent harm.
عطن ('atn): A well specifically dug for watering camels and livestock.
ناضح (nadih): A well used for irrigating fields, or the camel used for drawing water for irrigation.
عين ('ayn): A natural spring or flowing water source.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the harim (protected area) for a natural spring ('ayn)?
A) 200 cubits
B) 300 cubits
C) 500 cubits
D) 600 cubits

Key Takeaway
The harim around a well or spring is a protected zone to prevent harm, and its size varies based on the purpose, with specific measurements established by hadith.

Hareem of Wells and Springs: Protected Perimeters Based on Usage
Defines the protected perimeter for different water sources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the hareem measurements for different water sources and identify the hadith evidence from the text.

Core Idea
The protected perimeter around a water source varies according to its purpose and size.

This lesson explores the Hanafi legal concept of hareem (protected perimeter) for wells and springs. By studying the hadith evidence cited in the commentary, you will learn the specific measurements for drinking wells, agricultural wells, flowing springs, and cultivated springs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the water source types and their hareem measurements
Read the passage and note the four categories: drinking well (25 cubits), agricultural well (50 cubits), flowing spring (300 cubits), and cultivated spring (600 cubits).
The commentary also mentions a general spring measurement of 500 cubits from Az-Zuhri.
2
Locate the hadith from Abu Huraira
The hadith from Sunan al-Darqutni and al-Bayhaqi is the primary evidence. Identify each measurement in the Arabic text.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut (single narrator) but accepted in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Analyze the additional athar from Bayhaqi
The athar adds 'on all its sides' clarifying that the hareem applies in all four directions.
This reinforces the perimeter concept.
4
Understand the reconciliation between hadith and athar
The hadith gives 600 cubits for a cultivated spring, but the commentary states a general spring hareem of 500 cubits from Az-Zuhri. The author gives priority to the 500-cubit figure for springs.
This shows how later authorities may choose among evidences.

الشرح الثميرى الجزء الثانى) ۴۲۹ كتاب احياء الموات عينا فحريمها خمس مائة ذراع فمن اراد ان يحفر بئرا في حريمها منع منه [ ١٧٠ ](۹) چالیس ہاتھ ہو گا۔ اور یہ چاروں طرف چالیس چالیس هاتھ ہوگا۔ اور کھیتی سیراب کرنے والے کنویں کے لئے پچاس هاتھ حریم ہو اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن ابي هريرة قال قال رسول الله الا الله حريم البئر البدى خمسة و عشرون ذراعا وحريم البئر العادية خمسون ذراعا وحريم العين السائحة ثلاث مائة ذراع وحريم عين الزرع ست مائة ذراع (الف) (دار قطني ، كتاب في : الاقضية والا حکام و غیر ذلک ، ج رابع ص ۱۴۱ نمبر ۴۴۷۳ رسنن بیہقی ، باب ما جاء فی حریم الآبار، ج سادس ہیں ۲۵۷، نمبر ۱۱۸۶۹) اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ پیر عادیہ یعنی کھیتی میں پانی پلانے والے کنویں کے لئے حریم پچاس هاتھ ہو گا۔ اور بیہقی کے اثر میں یہ بھی اضافہ ہے نواحيها كلها جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ کنویں کے چاروں طرف پچاس پچاس هاتھ حریم ہونا چاہئے (۲) یوں بھی کھیتی سیراب کرنے کے لئے اونٹ چاروں طرف گھومتا ہے۔ اس لئے چاروں طرف پچاس پچاس هاتھ حریم چاہئے اور چشمے کا حریم پانچ سو هاتھ ہو اس کی ایک دلیل اوپر کی حدیث گزری حریم عين الزرع ست مائة ذراع اور دوسرا اثر یہ ہے وقال الزهرى وسمعت الناس يقولون حريم العيون خمسمائة ذراع (ب) (سن للمبتی ، باب ما جاء فی حریم الآبار، ج سادس ص ۲۵۷ نمبر ۱۱۸۶۹) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ چشمے کے لئے پانچ سو هاتھ حریم ہونا چاہئے۔ انت عطن : اونٹ کو پانی پلانے کا کنواں، ناضح : کھیتی سیراب کرنے کا کنواں، یا وہ اونٹ جس سے کھیتی سیراب کی جاتی ہے۔
(Book of Reviving Dead Lands) For a spring, its hareem is five hundred cubits. Whoever wants to dig a well within its hareem is prevented. [170](9) It will be forty cubits. And this will be forty cubits on all four sides. For a well used for irrigating cultivation, its hareem is fifty cubits. The proof for this is the hadith: from Abu Huraira who said: The Messenger of Allah said: 'Indeed, the hareem of a well for drinking is twenty-five cubits, and the hareem of an agricultural well is fifty cubits, and the hareem of a flowing spring is three hundred cubits, and the hareem of a cultivated spring is six hundred cubits.' (Dar Qutni, Book of Judgments and Rulings, vol. 4 p. 141 no. 4473; Sunan Bayhaqi, Chapter on Hareem of Wells, vol. 6 p. 257 no. 11869). From this it is known that for a well 'adīya, i.e., a well used for irrigating cultivation, the hareem is fifty cubits. And in the athar of Bayhaqi there is the addition 'on all its sides', from which it is known that from all sides of the well, fifty cubits each is the hareem. (2) Likewise, because the camel used for irrigation goes around all sides, so fifty cubits on all sides is the hareem. For a spring, its hareem is five hundred cubits. One proof is the aforementioned hadith: 'the hareem of a cultivated spring is six hundred cubits', and the second athar is: Az-Zuhri said, 'I heard the people saying: the hareem of springs is five hundred cubits' (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Chapter on Hareem of Wells, vol. 6 p. 257 no. 11869). From this athar it is known that for a spring, the hareem is five hundred cubits. 'Ațan: a well for watering camels; nāḍiḥ: a well for irrigating cultivation, or the camel used for irrigation.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof defining the hareem (protected perimeter) for different types of water sources in Hanafi law. The text begins by stating the rule for a spring: its hareem is five hundred cubits (ذراع), and anyone who wants to dig a well within that perimeter is prevented. Then the commentary expands on this by explaining the hareem for wells: a well for drinking (بئر بدی) has a hareem of 25 cubits, an agricultural well (بئر عادية) has a hareem of 50 cubits. The proof is a hadith from Abu Hurairah in which the Prophet ﷺ specifies these measurements. The commentator also cites an athar from Bayhaqi adding that the hareem is on all four sides. For a spring (عين), two measurements are given: one from the hadith (600 cubits for a cultivated spring) and one from the saying of Az-Zuhri (500 cubits). The commentator reconciles these by stating the hareem as 500 cubits. The passage also defines terms: 'atan' is a well for watering camels, and 'nadiḥ' is a well for irrigating cultivation. The key point is that the hareem varies according to the purpose and size of the water source, and it is a protected area that cannot be encroached upon.

Key Terms
حریم (harīm): The protected perimeter around a water source within which no one else may dig or construct without permission.
بئر بدی (bi'r badī): A well for drinking water, also called a 'village well'.
بئر عادیة (bi'r 'ādiyya): An agricultural well used for irrigating crops.
عین سائحة ('ayn sā'iḥa): A naturally flowing spring on the ground surface.
عین زرع ('ayn zar'): A spring used for irrigation of cultivated land.

Key Takeaway
The hareem of a water source is not uniform; it varies according to its use and size, as established by the hadith and further clarified by later authorities.

Reviving Land Abandoned by a River: The Condition of Possible Return
Ruling on reviving land where a river has changed course

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on reviving a riverbed by distinguishing when it is permissible and when it is prohibited based on the possibility of water return.

Core Idea
A riverbed abandoned by water is not dead land if the water may return; it remains a public pathway.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi land law: when a river changes course and leaves its old bed dry. The ruling balances the public interest in the river's natural flow with the potential for reclaiming barren land. By the end, you will understand the key factor that determines whether such land can be revived and owned.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal category
The act is reviving (ihya) land that was once a riverbed. In Hanafi fiqh, this falls under the rules of dead land (mawat). Determine the general hukm: revival is normally permissible with Imam's permission.
2
Recognize the decisive condition
The passage presents a crucial condition: whether the water might return to its original bed. This overrides the general permissibility. Assess the likelihood of return, not the current state.
The test is possibility, not actuality.
3
Apply the condition to two scenarios
If return is possible (e.g., seasonal flooding), revival is prohibited (haram) because the bed is a public necessity for water flow. If return is impossible (e.g., permanent river shift), the land becomes like dead land (mawat) and revival is permissible (mubah).
The passage uses the Euphrates and Tigris as examples.
4
Check additional requirements
Even when revival is permissible, additional conditions apply: the land must not be within another's harim (protected perimeter), and the reviver must obtain the Imam's permission. Without these, ownership does not transfer.
Harim refers to the buffer zone around existing property or water sources.
5
Synthesize the ruling
Combine the condition and requirements: revival of an abandoned riverbed is only lawful if (1) water return is impossible, (2) no prior rights exist, and (3) Imam's permission is granted. Otherwise, the land remains public property.
This balances individual ownership with public interest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۶۷۰] (۹) جوز مین فرات اور دجلہ نہر نے چھوڑ دی اور پانی اس سے ہٹ گیا۔ پس اگر اس کا اس طرف لوٹنا ممکن ہو تو اس کا آباد کرنا جائز نہیں ہے۔ ر فرات یاد جلہ ندی مثلا ایک جگہ سے بہ رہی تھی۔ اور وہاں چھوڑ کر دوسری جگہ بہنا شروع کر دیا تو اندازہ لگائے کہ دوبارہ اپنی جگہ پر آنے کا انداز ہے یا نہیں۔ اگر دوبارہ اپنی جگہ پر آنے کا انداز ہے تو اس جگہ کو آباد کرنے کے لئے دینا جائز نہیں ہے۔ نہر بہنے کے لئے چاہئے ورنہ اتنا پانی کس راستے سے جائے گا۔ اس لئے یہ عوام کے فائدے کی جگہ ہے اس لئے اس کو آباد کرنے نہ دی جائے۔ اور اگر دوبارہ اس جگہ پر آنے کا امکان نہ ہو تو وہ زمین موات کی طرح ہے۔ اگر کسی کا اس کے ساتھ حق متعلق نہ ہو اور امام کی اجازت سے اس کو آباد کرے تو وہ اس کا مالک ہو جائے گا۔ [۱۹۷۱] (۱۰) اور اگر نہیں ممکن ہے کہ اس کی طرف لوٹے تو وہ موات کی زمین کی طرح ہے۔ اگر کسی آباد کرنے والے کا حریم نہ ہو تو اس کا مالک لعامر يملكه من احیاه باذن الامام
[1670] (9) As for the Euphrates and Tigris, if they leave a place and the water diverts from it, then if it is possible for it to return, it is not permissible to revive that land. For example, the Euphrates or Tigris rivers used to flow in one place, and then they left it and began flowing elsewhere. One should assess whether it is likely to return to its original place. If there is a chance of return, then giving that land for revival is not permissible because the river needs a path for its water; otherwise, so much water would have no passage. Therefore, it is a place of public benefit, so it should not be given for revival. And if there is no possibility of return, then that land is like dead land. If no one else has a right to it, and one revives it with the permission of the Imam, then he becomes its owner. [1971] (10) And if it is not possible for it to return, then it is like dead land. If there is no hareem of any person associated, then its owner becomes the one who revives it with the permission of the Imam.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on the status of land that a river has abandoned after changing its course. The key distinction is whether the water might return to its original bed. If the return is possible, the land cannot be claimed for revival because it is considered a public pathway for the river. The river's old bed must be kept open for the water to flow again, preventing anyone from owning it. If, however, there is no possibility of the water returning (e.g., the river has permanently shifted), then the old bed becomes like dead land (موات). In that case, a person may revive it with the Imam's permission and become the owner, provided no one else has a prior right (such as a hareem boundary). The passage also mentions that the reviver must not encroach on another's hareem. This ruling balances public interest in the river's natural course with the possibility of bringing barren land into cultivation.

Key Terms
موات (mawāt): Dead, uncultivated, ownerless land that can be revived with Imam's permission.
حریم (harīm): The protected perimeter around an existing property or water source that cannot be encroached upon.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition can abandoned river land be revived?
A) When the river has permanently changed course
B) When the land is fertile
C) When the Imam owns the land
D) When no one objects

Key Takeaway
The decisive factor in reviving an abandoned riverbed is whether the water may return; if it may, the land is reserved for public benefit; if not, it becomes dead land subject to revival with Imam's permission.

Hareem of a Canal in Another's Land: Dispute of Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on canal hareem

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the hareem of a canal in another's land and identify the practical consequence of their disagreement.

Core Idea
A canal in another's land does not automatically grant a hareem — only explicit evidence suffices according to Abu Hanifa, while the Sahibayn see a narrow path as a necessary accessory.

This passage examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: when a canal is dug in another's property, does the canal owner automatically gain the right to a strip of adjacent land (hareem)? Imam Abu Hanifa insists on explicit proof; the Sahibayn argue that the canal's function necessitates a narrow pathway. This disagreement highlights the tension between textual strictness and practical necessity in Hanafi property law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Determine the exact question: does a canal owner have a right to a hareem (protected perimeter) in someone else's land without explicit proof?
The issue is about property rights, not worship.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Imam Abu Hanifa holds no hareem without clear evidence (bayyinah). His proof: hadith specifies hareem only for springs and wells, not canals; general principle requires proof for any right over another's property.
He rejects analogy from wells to canals because the texts are specific.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani argue that a narrow path (musannāh) is a necessary accessory. Their proof: a canal cannot function without a path for walking and depositing dirt; analogy to springs which have a hareem according to hadith.
They invoke the principle that proof of a thing includes its necessities.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa counters that the analogy is invalid because the hadith is limited to springs; extending it to canals would infringe on ownership without textual basis.
This shows the school's methodological difference: textual strictness vs. maslahah (necessity).
5
Determine the practical difference
Under Abu Hanifa, the canal owner gets no land beyond the canal itself unless proven. Under the Sahibayn, a narrow path is automatically included.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

اجازت سے۔ دجلہ یا فرات جیسی ندی کا پانی ہٹ گیا تھا اور دوبارہ اس جگہ پر آنے کا امکان نہیں ہے تو وہ موات زمین کی طرح ہے۔ جو اس کو آباد کرے گا وہ اس کا مالک ہو جائے گا۔ لیکن اس میں دو شرطیں ہیں۔ ایک تو دوسرے کی زمین میں کسی کی نہر کا ثبوت ہوا تو نہر کی ثبوت کی وجہ سے کیا اس کو حریم کی جگہ بھی مل جائے گی یا نہیں ؟ تو امام ابوحنیفہ فرماتے ہیں کہ بغیر بینہ کے حریم کی جگہ نہیں ملے گی۔ دوسرے کی زمین ہے اس لئے نہر کے ثبوت سے حریم کا ثبوت ہونا ضروری نہیں جب تک کہ اس کے لئے بعینہ نہ ہو (۲) اور چونکہ حدیث میں کنویں اور چشمے کے لئے حریم کا ثبوت ہے، نہر کے لئے حریم کا ثبوت نہیں ہے اس لئے ایک کو دوسرے پر لوازم میں سے ہے۔ ورنہ نہر والا پانی پلانے کے لئے چلے گا کہاں؟ نہر کی مٹی کھودنی ہوگی تو کہاں ڈالے گا۔ اس لئے پیلی پڑی اور بند تو لازمی طور پر دینا ہو گا جو اس کی ضرورت کی ہو۔ کسی چیز کا ثبوت اس کے
