کے لئے تین سو هاتھ اور چھ سو هاتھ حریم ہے۔ تو نہر بھی چشمے کی قسم ہے اس لئے اس کے لئے بھی حریم کا ثبوت ہوگا۔ حاشیہ : (الف) زمین پر بہنے والے چشمے کا حریم تین سو هاتھہ ہے۔ اور کاشتکاروں کے چشمے کا تریم چھ سو هاتھ ہے۔
[2] 11 Whoever has a canal in another's land, he does not have a hareem according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, unless he has clear evidence for it. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he has a bank (مسناة) of the canal to walk on and to throw his dirt. It will happen if it is revived with the Imam's permission. For example, the water of the Tigris or Euphrates left and there is no possibility of return, then it is like dead land. Whoever revives it will be its owner. But there are two conditions: first, that place must not be the hareem of anyone else, such as a bund etc.; second, that it is revived with the Imam's permission. The condition of Imam's permission has passed before. And that it is not another's hareem, because if someone has possession, it is not considered dead land. [1672] (11) Whoever has a canal in another's land, he has no hareem according to Abu Hanifa unless he has explicit proof. According to the two, he has a narrow path of the canal on which he can walk and throw dirt. One case is when he digs a canal in dead land. Then the canal automatically has a hareem, otherwise where would he put the dirt? Here the issue is: if a canal is established in another's land, does he also get a hareem place due to the canal's proof? Abu Hanifa says without clear evidence, he will not get a hareem place. Because it is another's land, so the proof of the canal does not necessitate the proof of hareem unless it is explicitly established. (2) And because in the hadith, hareem is proven for wells and springs, not for canals, so we will not establish hareem in another's land by analogy. The principle is that hareem cannot be established on another's land without evidence. The two say that once the canal is proven, a narrow path is among its necessities. Otherwise, where would the owner walk? Where would he throw the dirt? Therefore, a narrow path and a bund are necessarily given as needed. The proof of a thing includes its necessities, so a path must be given. (2) The hadith has passed: 'the hareem of a flowing spring is 300 cubits, and the hareem of a cultivated spring is 600 cubits' (Darqutni, vol. 4 p. 142 no. 4473). So a canal is a type of spring, so it should also have a hareem. Footnote: (a) The hareem of a flowing spring is 300 cubits, and the hareem of a cultivated spring is 600 cubits.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether a canal (نهر) dug in another person's land has a hareem (protected perimeter). Abu Hanifa holds that the owner of the canal has no hareem in someone else's land unless he can prove a specific right to it (e.g., by evidence). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that the canal owner is entitled to a narrow path (مسناة) along the canal for walking and for depositing excavated dirt, as this is a necessary accessory to the canal. The commentary elaborates the reasoning: Abu Hanifa emphasizes that a canal in another's land does not automatically grant a hareem because the land belongs to another, and general principles require explicit proof for any right over another's property. The Sahibayn, on the other hand, consider the path a logical necessity; without it, the canal would be unusable. They also draw analogy to springs, which have a hareem according to hadith, and argue that a canal is similar. However, Abu Hanifa restricts the analogy because the hadith specifically mentions springs, not canals. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated in the text, but the dispute is acknowledged. This case illustrates the Hanafi method of balancing textual evidence (hadith) with rational necessity (istihsan) in property law.

Key Terms
مسناة (musannāh): A narrow bank or pathway along a canal for walking and depositing mud, considered a necessary accessory by the Sahibayn.
حریم (harīm): Protected perimeter; here, the right to a strip of land adjacent to a canal.
بینة (bayyinah): Clear evidence or proof, such as a document or witness testimony, required by Abu Hanifa to establish hareem in another's land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is required for a canal owner to have a hareem in another's land?
A) Permission from the landowner
B) Clear evidence (bayyinah)
C) The canal must be for irrigation
D) The land must be dead

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi property law, no right over another's land is presumed without evidence, but the Sahibayn grant an exception for the essential accessory of a canal path.

Reviving Land from Riverbed: Ownership Conditions
Ruling on reviving riverbed land with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for owning riverbed land that has become dry and apply them to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Reviving dead land requires Imam's permission and the land must not be another's harem.

In this passage, we examine the Hanafi ruling on land that was once a riverbed (like the Tigris or Euphrates) but from which the water has permanently receded. Such land is treated as dead land (mawat). However, two conditions must be satisfied for the reviver to become the owner: the land must not be within the protected perimeter (harem) of another's property, and the reviver must obtain the Imam's permission. We will explore these conditions and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is reviving dead land (ihya al-mawat). The ruling is that the reviver becomes the owner if conditions are met.
The base ruling is stated in the matn: 'yamlukhu man ahyahu bi-idhni al-imam'.
2
Recognize the specific case
The passage applies this to a riverbed like the Tigris or Euphrates where water has permanently receded. Such land is considered dead land.
This is an application of the general rule to a specific scenario.
3
List the first condition
The land must not be part of the harem (protected perimeter) of another's property, e.g., someone's dam or well enclosure.
Harem is defined by Hanafi fiqh for various structures.
4
List the second condition
The reviver must have obtained the Imam's permission (idhn al-imam) before reviving.
The Imam here represents the ruler or state authority.
5
Understand the consequence
If either condition is missing, the reviver does not become the owner. The land remains dead or belongs to another.
The passage emphasizes that possession by another excludes it from dead land.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Book of Reviving Dead Lands: For the one who revives it, he owns it with the permission of the Imam. (Urdu:) It will become his if he has developed it with the permission of the Imam. If the water of a river like the Tigris or Euphrates has receded and there is no possibility of it returning, then it is like dead land. Whoever develops it will become its owner. But there are two conditions. One: that the place is not the harem of any developer, e.g., someone's dam, etc. And the second condition: that he has developed it with the permission of the Imam. The condition of the Imam's permission has already passed. And that it should not be the harem of another, because if someone has possession over it, then that land is not considered dead.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage states a ruling from the subject of reviving dead lands (ihya al-mawat). The Arabic matn says: the one who revives dead land becomes its owner if he does so with the permission of the Imam. The Urdu commentary explains that this applies to land that was formerly a riverbed, like that of the Tigris or Euphrates, where the water has permanently receded. Such land is treated as dead land. However, two conditions must be met: (1) the land must not be within the harem (protected perimeter) of another's property, meaning it should not be, for example, part of someone's embankment or dam, and (2) the reviver must have obtained the Imam's permission. The commentary reiterates that possession by another would exclude it from the category of dead land.

Key Terms
موات (mawāt): Land that is not owned by anyone and is not being used or cultivated.
حریم (ḥarīm): The protected perimeter around a developed property, well, spring, etc., that is considered part of the property for necessary uses.
احیاء (iḥyā’): The act of developing dead land, such as by digging a well, building, or planting, which makes it productive.

Harem of a Canal in Another's Land: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on automatic harem rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether a canal owner gets a harem in another's land, identify the proofs and principle (lawazim) used by each side, and apply the thamarah al-khilaf to a real scenario.

Core Idea
Proof of a canal does not automatically prove a harem in another's land without evidence.

This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: when a person has a canal (nahr) in another's land, does he automatically own a narrow bank (masnat) for walking and mud disposal? Imam Abu Hanifa says no – explicit evidence is required. The two companions (Sahibayn) say yes – the canal's proof necessitates its accessories. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each view and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's Position
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that a canal owner has no harem in another's land unless he produces clear evidence (bayyina) proving it.
The burden of proof is on the claimant.
2
Identify the Opposing View (Sahibayn)
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that a narrow bank (masnat) is automatically granted as a necessary accessory (lazim) of the canal.
They consider this essential for access and mud disposal.
3
Analyze the Proofs of Each Side
Abu Hanifa: hadith only specify harem for wells and springs, not canals; analogy (qiyas) cannot override ownership. Sahibayn: proof of a thing includes its necessities; canal is like a spring, so spring hadith applies by analogy.
Focus on the principle of lawazim (necessities) vs. specificity of evidence.
4
Examine the Hanafi Response to Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa's response: hadith on springs is specific, not analogizable; necessities do not transfer ownership without proof; the land is owned by another.
The key distinction is that qiyas is restricted when it overrides property rights.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Practical difference: according to Abu Hanifa, without evidence the canal owner has no right to any bank on another's land. According to Sahibayn, he automatically has a narrow bank for access and mud.
This affects litigation and property boundaries.

Source Text

And whoever has a canal in another's land, he has no harem according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, unless he has clear evidence for it. But according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he has a narrow bank of the canal upon which he can walk and upon which he can place its mud. (Urdu:) The one who has a canal in another's land, he has no harem according to Imam Abu Hanifa, unless it is specifically established. According to the two companions, he will have a narrow pathway for the canal on which he can walk and on which he can throw mud. First: if he digs a canal in dead land, then a harem is necessary with the canal, otherwise where will he put the mud? The issue here is that when a canal is proven in another's land, will he also get a place for harem due to the proof of the canal? Imam Abu Hanifa says that without evidence, the place of harem will not be obtained. Because it is another's land, proof of the canal does not necessitate proof of harem unless it is specifically for that purpose. And since in hadith there is proof of harem for wells and springs, and not for canals, we will not establish harem in another's land by analogizing one to the other. His principle is that harem will not be established on another's land without evidence. The two companions say: when the canal is proven, then a narrow pathway is among its necessities. Otherwise, where will the canal owner walk to water? And when he needs to excavate mud, where will he put it? Therefore, a narrow path and a dam must be given as needed. The proof of a thing includes its necessities, so a pathway must be given. The hadith has already passed: 'The harem of a flowing spring is three hundred cubits, and the harem of a spring for cultivation is six hundred cubits' (al-Daraqutni). So a canal is also a type of spring, so harem would be proven for it as well. Footnote: (a) The harem of a spring flowing on the ground is three hundred cubits, and of a spring for farmers is six hundred cubits. Their principle is that if something is established, its necessities are automatically established because they are indispensable. 'Nata' (bang) is a dam to stop floodwater.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning whether a person who has a canal (nahr) in another's land is entitled to a harem (protected perimeter) around it. The Arabic matn states the ruling: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the canal owner has no harem in that land unless he has explicit evidence (bayyina) proving it. According to the two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, he is entitled to a narrow bank (masnat) upon which he can walk and deposit mud from the canal. The Urdu commentary expands on the reasoning. Imam Abu Hanifa's argument: since the land belongs to another, the proof of the canal does not automatically prove a harem; rather, it requires specific evidence. Moreover, hadith only mentions harem for wells and springs, not for canals, so analogy (qiyas) cannot be used to extend it to another's land. The two companions argue that the proof of the canal necessitates its accessories (lawazim); otherwise, the canal owner would have no way to walk for irrigation or to deposit excavated mud. They cite the principle that the proof of a thing includes its necessities. They further invoke hadith regarding springs to analogize that a canal, being similar to a spring, should also have a harem. The passage concludes with a principle that if something is established, its necessities are automatically established. Thus, the dispute hinges on whether the harem is a necessary accessory (lazim) or requires separate proof.

Key Terms
حریم (ḥarīm): The protected area around a water source necessary for its maintenance and access.
مسناة (masnāt): A narrow embankment or bank of a canal used for walking and depositing mud.
بینة (bayyina): Clear evidence or proof, often two male witnesses or equivalent.
لوازم (lawāzim): Necessary accessories or consequences that automatically accompany something when proven.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In another's land, proof of a canal does not automatically grant a harem – explicit evidence is needed according to Imam Abu Hanifa, though Sahibayn argue for automatic accessory rights.

The Scope of Limited Permission: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Dispute on the scope of limited permission for a slave to trade

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a slave permitted in one type of trade becomes ma'dhun in all types, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Permission establishes general capacity for trade, not just specific authorization.

This lesson examines a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: when a master permits his slave to trade in only one type of commodity, does the slave gain the capacity to trade in all types? The answer determines the validity of the slave's transactions beyond the specified type. We will analyze the Hanafi reasoning based on the nature of legal capacity (ahliyyah) and contrast it with the Shafi'i textual approach.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling: the slave is considered ma'dhun (permitted) in all types of trade, even if the master's permission was limited to one type.
This is stated clearly in the matn: 'he is ma'dhun in all of them'.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the slave is ma'dhun only in the specific type mentioned; any transaction in another type is unauthorized and invalid.
The opposing view is attributed to al-Shafi'i in the commentary.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Al-Shafi'i argues that permission is an act of the master, so it is restricted to what he explicitly allows. Other types are not included without clear authorization.
This is a literal reading of the master's statement.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds that before permission, the slave had no capacity (ahliyyah) to trade. Granting permission for one type creates the capacity for trade itself, which is indivisible and covers all types. Restricting it would contradict the purpose of permission.
The commentary emphasizes that ahliyyah is a single legal attribute.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi, the slave's transactions in other types are valid and binding on the master (within limits); under Shafi'i, they are invalid unless the master later ratifies them.
This difference affects the enforceability of contracts made by the slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۶۷٥] (۳) وان اذن له فى نوع منها دون غيره فهو ماذون في جميعها
یہ سب کام تجارت کے معاون ہیں اور تجارت میں ان کی ضرورت پڑتی ہے اس لئے یہ سب کام غلام کر سکتا ہے۔ [۱۶۷٥] (۳) اگر اس کو اجازت دی اس میں سے ایک قسم کی نہ کہ دوسرے کی تو اس کو اجازت ہوگی اس کے تمام میں۔ مولی نے کسی ایک قسم کی چیز میں تجارت کرنے کی اجازت دی تو تمام چیزوں کی تجارت کی اہمیت ہو جائے گی۔ تجارت کی اہمیت ہونا اور چیز ہے۔ البتہ تجارت اسی چیز کی کرے گا جس کی مولی نے کہا ہے۔ اصل یہ ہے کہ مولی کی اجازت سے پہلے غلام میں تجارت کرنے کی اہلیت نہیں تھی ۔ جب اس نے ایک قسم میں تجارت کی اجازت دی تو تمام قسموں کی تجارت کی اہمیت ہوگئی اور وہ تمام قسموں میں ماذون سمجھا جائے گا۔ اور اس کی خرید و فروخت کرے گا تو نافذ ہو جائے گا۔ یہ اور بات ہے کہ مصلحت کے خلاف ہو یا مولی کا نقصان ہو تو متعینہ چیز کے علاوہ کی تجارت نہ کرے۔
نانه امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ اجازت مولی کی جانب سے موصول ہوتی ہے اس لئے وہ جس خاص چیز کی تجارت کی اجازت دی ہے اسی میں مازون ہو گا باقی چیزوں میں ماذون نہیں ہوگا۔
[1675] (3) And if he gives him permission for one type (of trade) but not another, he is ma'dhun in all of them. All these actions are ancillary to trade and needed in trade, so the slave can do them. (3) If he gives him permission for one type, he will have permission in all. When the master gives permission for one type of commodity, the importance of trade in all types will be established. The importance of trade is one thing; nevertheless, he will only trade in what the master indicated. The principle is that before the master's permission, the slave had no capacity to trade. When he gave permission for one type, the capacity for all types came into being, and he will be considered ma'dhun in all types. His buying and selling will be effective. However, if it is against interest or causes loss to the master, he should not trade beyond the specified item. But Imam al-Shafi'i says that permission is received from the master, so he will be ma'dhun only in the specific thing for which permission was given; he will not be ma'dhun in other things.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the scope of a limited permission to trade. The matn states that if a master permits the slave to trade in one type of commodity, the slave is considered ma'dhun in all types. The commentary explains the reasoning: permission bestows upon the slave the general capacity (ahliyyah) for trade; once that capacity exists, it applies to all types, although prudence dictates that he should stick to the master's specified item to avoid loss. The Shafi'i position is that permission is specific and thus limited to the stated type only. The Hanafi response is that the nature of trade requires a general capacity; otherwise, the slave's actions outside that type would be invalid, which contradicts the purpose of permission. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the Hanafi one.

Key Terms
اهمية (ahmiyyah): Legal capacity or competence to engage in trade that is acquired through permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) He is ma'dhun only for cloth trade.
B) He is ma'dhun for all types of trade.
C) He is not considered ma'dhun at all.
D) His permission is void if limited.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school sees permission as granting the underlying capacity for trade, not just a specific license, so limited permission nonetheless extends to all types.

The Ma'dhun's Prohibition from Marriage and Marriage Arrangements
Ruling: Ma'dhun prohibited from marriage and arranging marriage of slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rationale for prohibiting the ma'dhun from marrying or arranging marriages of his slaves, and state the intra-Hanafi difference reported from Abu Yusuf regarding marrying off a slave-girl.

Core Idea
Marriage is not trade; a ma'dhun cannot incur obligations that harm the master.

This lesson examines the limit of a ma'dhun's authority over personal and slave marriages. While a ma'dhun can trade, entering into marriage or arranging marriage for his slaves is prohibited because it imposes maintenance expenses and falls outside the scope of trade. A dissent from Imam Abu Yusuf, who permits marrying off a slave-girl due to potential benefit, introduces an important intra-Hanafi nuance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the ma'dhun marrying himself or arranging marriages for his male and female slaves. The ruling is prohibition (haram).
Prohibition is for both his own marriage and arranging slaves' marriages.
2
Understand the rationale
Marriage imposes an obligation to provide maintenance (nafaqah), which is a loss for the master. Also, marriage is not a requisite or auxiliary of trade (al-tijārah).
The ma'dhun's permission is limited to trade-related acts.
3
Apply to own marriage
The ma'dhun cannot marry because it is not part of trade and causes financial loss to the master through nafaqah.
Same reasoning applies to arranging his slaves' marriages.
4
Note Abu Yusuf's exception
Imam Abu Yusuf holds that marrying off a slave-girl is permissible because it brings benefit: the mahr from the husband and offspring who become slaves of the ma'dhun (hence the master).
This is a valid ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is Abu Hanifa's view of complete prohibition, as it is the established position of the school (zahir al-riwayah).
The text does not explicitly prefer one, but the default is Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس له ان يتزوج ولا ان يزوج مماليكه [۱۷۹]
[۱۹۷۸] (۶) مازون کے لئے جائز نہیں ہے کہ شادی کرے اور نہ یہ کہ اپنے مملوک کی شادی کرائے۔ غلام کی شادی کرنے سے نقصان ہے۔ کیونکہ نان نفقہ ادا کرنا ہوگا۔ نیز یہ تجارت میں سے نہیں ہے اس لئے خود کی شادی نہیں کر سکتا۔ اور یہی نقصان مملوک کی شادی کرانے میں ہے۔ اس لئے اپنے مملوک غلام باندی کی بھی شادی نہیں کر سکتا ۔ نیز یہ تجارت کے لوازمات یا معاون نہیں ہے اس لئے بھی نہیں کروا سکتا۔
امام ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں باندی کی شادی کرانے سے بچہ ہوگا جو باندی اور غلام ہوں گے اور باندی کا بھی مہر آئے گا جو فائدے کی چیز ہے اس لئے ماذون غلام اپنی باندی کی شادی کر اسکتا ہے۔
And for him it is not permissible to marry nor to marry off his slaves. [1978] (6) It is not permissible for the ma'dhun to marry nor to arrange the marriage of his own slaves. There is loss in a slave marrying because he would have to provide maintenance. Also, this is not part of trade, so he cannot marry himself. The same loss is in arranging marriage of his slaves. Therefore, he cannot arrange marriage of his male or female slaves. Also, it is not a requisite or auxiliary of trade, so he cannot do it. Imam Abu Yusuf says: Arranging marriage of a slave-girl results in children who become slaves and also the mahr of the slave-girl which is a benefit, so the ma'dhun slave can marry off his slave-girl.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that a ma'dhun (slave permitted to trade) cannot enter into marriage nor arrange the marriage of his own slaves. The reasoning provided in the Urdu commentary is that marriage imposes financial obligations (maintenance) which harm the master's interest, and marriage is not among the activities necessary for trade. It further notes that Imam Abu Yusuf holds a dissenting view regarding a slave-girl: because her marriage brings benefit (offspring and mahr), it is permissible for the ma'dhun to marry her off. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
ماذون (mā'dhūn): A slave who has been granted permission (ijāzah) by his master to engage in trade.
مملوك (mamlūk): Slave owned by the ma'dhun (as he may own property).
نفقہ (nafaqah): Maintenance expenses, especially food and clothing for dependents.

D) Because marriage is a form of worship, not trade

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun's permission is limited to trade; marriage and its arrangements are prohibited because they impose financial obligations beyond trade and cause loss to the master.

Limits of a Ma'dhun's Trade Permission
Ruling: Ma'dhun prohibited from kitaba and itq 'alā māl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling regarding a ma'dhun's inability to make kitaba or free a slave for payment and explain why it is prohibited.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun cannot use his trade permission for non-trade profits.

This lesson addresses a specific limitation on the ma'dhun slave who has been granted permission to trade. While he can engage in buying and selling, not all profit-generating activities are permitted. The text explains that making a kitaba contract or freeing a slave for payment is prohibited because these are not considered part of trade.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agent and acts
The agent is a ma'dhun slave. The acts are: (1) making a kitaba contract with his own slave, (2) freeing a slave for payment (itq 'alā māl).
Kitaba involves installment payment for freedom; itq 'alā māl is a one-time payment for freedom.
2
State the ruling
Both acts are prohibited (haram) for the ma'dhun. The text uses a negative imperative: 'لا يكاتب ولا يعتق على مال'.
3
Understand the rationale
Although these acts bring monetary gain, they are not among the 'requisites or auxiliaries of trade.' The ma'dhun's permission is limited to trade and its directly related activities.
Trade auxiliaries include actions like hiring help, renting storage, etc.
4
Contrast with permitted trade
Ordinary sale and purchase are allowed because they form the core of trade. Kitaba and itq 'alā māl are seen as independent transactions unrelated to commodity trading.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

مازون غلام اپنے غلام کو مکاتب نہیں بنا سکتا اور نہ مال کے بدلے آزاد کر سکتا ہے۔
اگر چہ اس صورت میں مال آئے گا لیکن چونکہ مکاتب بنانا اور مال کے بدلے آزاد کرنا تجارت کے لوازم یا معاون میں سے نہیں ہیں۔اس لئے ماذون غلام یہ نہیں کر سکتا۔

The ma'dhun slave cannot make his slave a mukatab nor can he free him for payment. Even though in such a case money would come, but because making a mukatab and freeing for payment are not among the requisites or auxiliaries of trade, therefore the ma'dhun slave cannot do this.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This ruling prohibits the ma'dhun from entering into a kitaba contract (where a slave buys his freedom in installments) or freeing a slave in exchange for payment. The commentary explains that although such actions bring monetary gain, they are not part of trade activities. The ma'dhun's permission is specifically for trade, and these are considered extraneous transactions that could harm the master's interest.

Key Terms
مکاتب (mukātab): A slave who has entered into a kitaba contract to purchase his freedom through installments.
عتق على مال (itq 'alā māl): Freeing a slave in exchange for a payment, similar to a sale of freedom.

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun slave's permission to trade does not extend to kitaba or freeing slaves for payment because these are not auxiliary to trade.

The Ma'dhun's Prohibition from Giving Gifts
Ruling statement with rational explanation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a ma'dhun giving gifts and explain the rational reasons for the prohibition.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun cannot give gifts because it causes loss or is outside the scope of trade.

This lesson examines the rule that a permissioned slave trader (ma'dhun) is prohibited from giving gifts, whether free or in exchange. Understanding this prohibition clarifies the boundaries of the ma'dhun's trading authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The ma'dhun is prohibited from giving gifts. Identify the two types mentioned: without compensation (hibah bighayri iwad) and with compensation (hibah bi iwad).
2
Analyze the reasoning for each type
For a gift without compensation, the reason is pure loss to the master. For a gift with compensation, the initial act is a gift (gratuitous) and the later exchange makes it not a clean trade; also gift-giving is not a requisite of trade.
3
Apply the underlying principle
The ma'dhun's permission is limited to trade transactions. Any act outside trade, like gifts, is invalid and prohibited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يهب بعوض ولا بغير عوض [ ١۶١ ]
[۱۶۸۰] (۸) اور نہ ہبہ کرے عوض سے اور نہ بغیر عوض کے ۔
بغیر عوض کے ہبہ کرنا مولی کو سراسر نقصان دیتا ہے اس لئے بغیر عوض کے ماذون ہبہ نہیں کر سکتا۔ اور عوض کے بدلے کرے تو اس میں ابتداء مفت ہے اور انتہاء بدلہ ہے اس لئے یہ بھی نہیں کر سکتا۔ نیز ہبہ کرنا لوازم تجارت میں سے نہیں ہے اس لئے نہیں کر سکتا۔
And he may not give a gift with compensation nor without compensation. [1680] (8) And he may not give a gift for a consideration nor without consideration. Giving without consideration causes pure loss to the master, so the ma'dhun cannot give a gift without consideration. And if he gives with consideration, initially it is free (gift) and later it is exchange, so he cannot do this either. Also, gift-giving is not among the requisites of trade, so he cannot do it.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This ruling prohibits the ma'dhun from making gifts (hiba) either for free or in exchange for something. The reasoning: a gift without return is pure loss to the master. A gift with exchange is problematic because the initial transfer is gratuitous; it is not a straightforward sale. Moreover, gifting is not a trade activity.

Key Terms
ہبہ (hibah): A gift; the transfer of ownership without compensation.
عوض (iwad): Compensation, consideration, or exchange given in return.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it starts as a gift (free) and ends as exchange
B) Because it requires a witness
C) Because the master must approve every gift
D) Because gifts are always prohibited for slaves

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun's authority is limited to trade; gifts are not trade and are therefore prohibited, whether free or with compensation.

Exception for Small Food Gifts and Hospitality
Exception to the ma'dhun's gift prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two scenarios where a ma'dhun may give gifts or hospitality and identify the conditions that make them permissible.

Core Idea
Small customary food gifts and reciprocal hospitality are exceptions to the ma'dhun's gift prohibition.

The ma'dhun (permitted trading slave or agent) is generally prohibited from giving away the owner's property. However, this lesson outlines a narrow exception: he may give small amounts of food as gifts or host someone who feeds him, provided the items are customary and small in scale. This exception supports trade and social norms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The default ruling is that a ma'dhun cannot give gifts from the owner's property because he is only permitted to trade.
Gifts are not part of trade.
2
State the exception scenarios
The exception allows two actions: giving a small food gift, and hosting someone who feeds him (reciprocal hospitality).
Both involve food and are small in quantity.
3
Understand the conditions
The gift must be small, consist of food, and follow social custom. Hospitality is only allowed in return for being fed.
The scale is 'little' (yaseer) and tied to custom.
4
Recognize the rationale
The reason (ta'lil) is that such gestures increase trade and attract customers, and they are socially accepted.
Trade benefit and custom legitimize the exception.
5
Distinguish from prohibited gifts
Large gifts, non-food items, or hospitality beyond custom are not covered by this exception and remain prohibited.
The exception is narrow and context-specific.

Source Text

These small things are under social permission, therefore they are allowed.

Commentary
This is an exception to the prohibition on gifts. The ma'dhun may give small amounts of food or show hospitality, especially if it helps his trade by attracting customers. Also, reciprocating hospitality from someone who feeds him is allowed. The scale is small and socially customary.

مہمانداری (mehmāndārī): Hospitality; hosting someone with food.

The Ma'dhun Slave's Person as Security for Trade Debts
Ruling: Debts incurred by ma'dhun are secured against his person; he may be sold.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the liability of a ma'dhun slave's person for trade debts and explain why his sale is permitted despite objections from Shafi'i and Zufar.

Core Idea
The ma'dhun slave's very person is security for his trade debts – he may be sold to satisfy creditors proportionally.

When a ma'dhun (permitted slave) engages in trade, any debts he incurs are not only attached to his earnings but also to his person (raqabah). If his earnings fall short, creditors can look to the slave himself as an asset. The Hanafi school upholds this principle, citing an athar from Ibrahim an-Nakha'i, while opponents like Imam Shafi'i and Imam Zufar argue that such a sale unjustly harms the master. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its evidence, and the practical differences between the schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the sale of a ma'dhun slave to satisfy debts incurred in trade. The ruling (hukm) is that such a sale is valid (sahih) and the proceeds are distributed proportionally among creditors.
The master may avoid the sale by paying the debts himself.
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites an athar (report) from Ibrahim an-Nakha'i: 'The slave is sold for debt even if it is more than his value.' This is a qawl sahabi (saying of a companion's student) and serves as the proof for the Hanafi position.
The athar is zanni al-thubut (speculative transmission) but qati al-dalala (definitive meaning).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit statement 'يُباع العبد في دين' directly permits selling the slave for debt. The phrase 'وإن كان أكثر من قيمته' underscores that even if the debt exceeds the slave's value, the sale remains valid.
This shows that the slave's person is the ultimate security for the debt.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Shafi'i and Imam Zufar oppose the sale, arguing that it causes loss (ضرر) to the master without corresponding benefit, since the master loses the slave's services.
This is an inter-madhhab dispute with practical consequences.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi response is that the trade debts were incurred for the benefit of the master (through permission to trade), so creditors must be satisfied. The athar provides direct textual support, making the Hanafi position the mufta bihi (operative ruling).
The proportional distribution (على الحصص) ensures fairness among creditors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وديونه متعلقة برقبته يباع فيها [ ۱۲۸۲ ( ۱۰ )
للغرماء الا ان يفديه المولى ويقسم ثمنه بينهم بالحصص [ ۱۲۸۳ (۱)
[۱۶۸۲) (۱۰) اور اس کے قرض متعلق ہوں گے ماذون کی گردن کے ساتھ ، وہ قرض خواہوں کے لئے بیچا جائے گا مگر یہ کہ مولی اس کا بدلہ دے دے۔ اور اس کی قیمت تقسیم کی جائے گی ان کے درمیان حصوں کے مطابق۔
تجارت کی وجہ سے جو کچھ قرض آیا یہ مازون غلام کی گردن پر ہوگا۔ جس کی وجہ سے پہلے غلام کے پاس جو کمایا ہوا مال ہے اس سے قرض ادا کیا جائے گا۔ اس سے بھی پورا نہ ہو تو غلام کو بیچ دیا جائے گا اور اس کی قیمت سے قرض والوں کا قرض ادا کیا جائے گا۔ اور وہ بھی قرض والوں کے حصے کے مطابق یعنی مثلا چار آدمیوں کے پانچ پانچ سو درہم قرض ہیں۔ اور قیمت میں ایک ہزار درہم آئے تو ہر ایک کو اس کے قرض کے آدھے حصے ملیں گے یعنی ہر ایک کو ڈھائی ڈھائی سو دیئے جائیں گے۔ ایک ہی قرض خواہ کو سب نہیں دے دیا جائے گا۔ اسی کو 'ہالحصص کہتے ہیں۔ تا کہ قرض والوں کا نقصان نہ ہو۔ ہاں مولی اپنی جیب سے قرض ادا کر دے تو اب غلام نہیں بیچا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ قرض والوں کو قرض مل گیا۔
عن ابراهيم قال يباع العبد في دين وان كان اكثر من قيمته (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب هل يباع العبد فی دینه ؟ ، ج ثامن ص ۲۸۵ نمبر ۱۵۲۳۷) اس اثر میں ہے کہ غلام کو دین میں بیچا جائے گا۔
امام شافعی اور امام زفر ماتے ہیں کہ قرض میں غلام نہیں بیچا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ اس سے مولی کا فائدہ کے بجائے نقصان ہوگا۔
And his debts are attached to his person; he is sold for them [1282 (10)] to the creditors unless the master redeems him, and his price is divided among them proportionally [1283 (1)]. [1682] (10) And his debts will be attached to the neck of the ma'dhun; he will be sold for the creditors, unless the master pays his compensation. And his price will be distributed among them according to shares. The debts incurred from trade will be on the neck of the ma'dhun slave. Therefore, first the wealth earned by the slave is used to pay debts. If that is insufficient, the slave is sold and his price is used to pay the creditors. And it is distributed proportionally, for example, four people are owed 500 dirhams each, and the price is 1000 dirhams, then each gets half of his debt, i.e., 250 each. It is not given to one creditor entirely. This is called 'ala al-hisas, so that the creditors do not suffer loss. Yes, if the master pays the debt from his own pocket, then the slave is not sold, because the creditors have received their due. From Ibrahim: The slave is sold for debt even if it is more than his value (Musannaf Abdurrazzaq, chapter on selling a slave for his debt, vol. 8, p. 285, no. 15237). In this athar, it is stated that the slave is sold for debt. Imam Shafi'i and Imam Zufar said that the slave will not be sold for debt, because that causes loss to the master rather than benefit.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage addresses the liability for debts incurred by the ma'dhun during trade. Such debts are first paid from the slave's own earnings. If that fails, the slave himself may be sold to satisfy creditors, with the proceeds distributed proportionally. The master has the option to pay the debts and prevent the sale. The text then cites an athar from Ibrahim supporting sale for debt, and notes that Imam Shafi'i and Imam Zufar disagree, arguing that selling causes loss to the master. This is an inter-madhhab dispute.

Key Terms
رقبہ (raqabah): The person of the slave; his physical being as an asset.
حساب حصوں (hisāb hisas): Proportional distribution among creditors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a ma'dhun's trade earnings are insufficient to cover his debts?
A) The master must pay the deficiency
B) The creditors forfeit the amount
C) The slave is sold to pay the debts proportionally
D) The slave is freed and debts are cancelled

Key Takeaway
Debts incurred by a ma'dhun slave in trade are secured against his person; he can be sold to satisfy them proportionally, though the master may prevent sale by paying the debts.

Residual Debt of a Ma'dhun Slave After Freedom
Ruling: Residual debt of ma'dhun pursued after freedom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the liability of a freed slave for debts incurred before sale as a ma'dhun and identify the principle protecting creditors.

Core Idea
The unpaid debt of a ma'dhun slave follows him even after freedom—the master is not liable.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi school concerning the liability for trade debts of a ma'dhun slave. When a ma'dhun is sold to pay his debts but the sale proceeds are insufficient, the remaining debt is not lost. The text explains that the creditors may pursue the debt after the slave gains freedom, and the master is not liable. This ruling is grounded in the principle of preventing loss to creditors as far as possible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act in question? The remaining debt of a ma'dhun slave. What is the ruling? It will be demanded from him after he becomes free.
The act is the liability for debt, not a physical action.
2
Identify the parties involved
Who is liable and who is exempt? The slave after freedom is liable; the master is not liable.
The master's liability is limited because he did not incur the debt and the slave has already been sold.
3
Understand the underlying principle
What general legal principle supports this ruling? The principle that the creditor should not suffer loss as far as possible (ḥattā al-imkān).
This principle overrides the potential loss of the debt by preserving the claim against the freed slave.
4
Apply to a similar scenario
Suppose the slave is never freed. According to the text, the debt is pursued after freedom; if he remains a slave, the creditors may have no recourse. (The text does not address this explicitly, but the ruling is conditional on freedom.)
The ruling is specifically about after freedom; other scenarios require qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان فضل من دیونه شيء طولب به بعد الحرية
[۱۶۸۳] (۱۱) پس اگر اس کے قرض میں سے کچھ بچ جائے تو اس کا مطالبہ کیا جائے گا آزادگی کے بعد ۔
قرض اتنا تھا کہ غلام کو بیچنے کے بعد جو قیمت آئی اس سے بھی قرض ادا نہیں ہوا بلکہ کچھ قرض باقی رہ گیا تو یہ قرض مولی سے وصول نہیں کیا جائے گا کیونکہ اس نے نہیں لیا ہے۔ اور اس کا غلام تو ایک مرتبہ بک چکا ہے۔ اور قرض والوں کا نقصان نہ ہو اس لئے یہی صورت باقی رہی کہ جب یہ ماذون غلام آزاد ہو اس وقت اس سے بقیہ قرض کا مطالبہ کیا جائے گا۔ اور اس سے وصول کرنے کی کوشش کی جائے گی۔ اصول یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ حتی الامکان قرض دینے والے کو نقصان نہ ہو۔
Then if there remains any of his debts, he will be pursued after freedom. [1683] (11) So if some of his debt remains, it will be demanded after he becomes free. The debt was such that after selling the slave, the price obtained did not even cover the debt; some debt remained. This debt will not be recovered from the master because he did not take it. And his slave has already been sold. To avoid loss to the creditors, the remaining course is that when this ma'dhun slave becomes free, then the remaining debt will be demanded from him, and efforts will be made to recover from him. The principle: This issue is based on the principle that the creditor should not suffer loss as far as possible.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This ruling addresses the situation where the sale of the ma'dhun slave does not fully satisfy his trade debts. The deficiency is not recoverable from the master, as the debt was incurred by the slave and the master has already lost the slave's value. Instead, the unpaid balance remains a liability on the slave, enforceable once he gains freedom. This protects creditors' rights while limiting the master's exposure.

Key Terms
حرية (hurriyyah): Freedom from slavery; the state of being free.
مطالبہ (mutālabah): Demand for payment; legal pursuit of debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The creditors lose the remainder
C) The freed slave is liable for the remainder
D) The remainder is cancelled by the court

Key Takeaway
The unpaid debts of a ma'dhun slave are not extinguished by his sale; they remain his personal liability, enforceable when he gains freedom.

Hijr on Ma'dhun: Public Knowledge as a Condition
Ruling with condition and rationale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for the effectiveness of hijr on a ma'dhun and apply it to scenarios involving partial knowledge.

Core Idea
A master's restriction only binds when the market knows.

In Hanafi fiqh, a ma'dhun slave is permitted to trade. However, the master may revoke this permission through hijr. This lesson examines the critical condition that makes hijr legally effective: public knowledge in the marketplace. Without it, transactions remain valid, protecting innocent traders.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and its ruling
Identify the act (hijr on ma'dhun) and the general principle stated: hijr is not effective until known in the market.
The ruling is a condition (shart) for legal effect.
2
Understand the rationale (talil)
Why is public knowledge required? To prevent loss to third parties who might deal with the slave in good faith.
The text explicitly gives the reason: 'lest they suffer loss'.
3
Determine the threshold of knowledge
The passage specifies that knowledge must reach 'most people in the market' (akthar ahli al-suq). One or two persons knowing is insufficient.
This is a quantitative threshold; 'most' implies a clear majority.
4
Apply the ruling during the gap period
If hijr is declared but not yet known, the slave's intermediate trades are valid and binding.
The hijr is suspended until public knowledge occurs.
5
Summarize the legal effect
After knowledge becomes widespread, any further trades by the slave are void. Before that, they are valid.
The hijr is not retroactive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان حجر عليه لم يصر محجورا عليه حتى يظهر الحجر بين اهل السوق
[۱۶۸۴) (۱۲) اگر اس پر حجر کیا تو اس پر حجر نہیں ہو گا یہاں تک کہ حجر ظاہر ہو جائے بازار والوں کے درمیان۔ شر مولی نے غلام مازون کو تجارت کرنے سے روک دیا اور حجر کردیا تو بازار کے اکثر لوگوں کو اس کا علم ہوتب حجر ہو گا۔ اگر ایک دو آدمی کو حجر کا علم ہوا تو ابھی حجر نہیں ہوگا۔ اس درمیان غلام نے تجارت کر لی تو نافذ ہو جائے گی۔
اگر اکثر لوگوں کو علم نہ ہو تو ممکن ہے کہ یہ سمجھ کر کہ ابھی اس پر حجر نہیں ہوا ہے اس سے خرید و فروخت کر لے اور ان کو نقصان ہو جائے۔ اس لئے اکثر لوگوں کو علم ہونا ضروری ہے۔
حجر : غلام کو تجارت کرنے سے منع کرنا۔
And if he (the master) places a restriction (hijr) upon him, he does not become restricted (mahjur) until the restriction becomes apparent among the people of the market. [1684] (12) If he places a restriction on him, the restriction will not take effect until it becomes known among the market people. Condition: When the master prevents the ma'dhun slave from trading and imposes hijr, then when most people in the market know about it, the hijr becomes effective. If only one or two people know, the hijr is not yet effective. If the slave trades in the meantime, it is valid. If most people do not know, it is possible that they might transact with him thinking he is still permitted, causing them loss. Therefore, it is necessary that most people know. Hijr: preventing the slave from trading.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This ruling stipulates that when a master revokes the ma'dhun's trade permission (hijr), this restriction only takes legal effect once it becomes widely known in the marketplace. The purpose is to protect third parties who might otherwise deal with the slave in good faith. If the knowledge is limited, transactions by the slave remain valid. This is a procedural safeguard.

Key Terms
حجر (hajr): Legal restriction or interdiction; here, the master's prohibition on the slave's trade.
ماذون (ma'dhun): Slave permitted to trade; his permission can be revoked.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does a master's restriction (hijr) on a ma'dhun take legal effect?
A) As soon as the master declares it privately
B) Only after informing a judge
C) When the majority of market people know about it
D) When the slave agrees

Key Takeaway
A master's hijr on a ma'dhun slave requires widespread public knowledge in the market; until then, the slave's trades remain valid.

Effect of Master's Incapacity on Ma'dhun's Permission
Ruling: Three events causing ma'dhun to become majur

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three events that turn a ma'dhun into a majur and explain why the slave's permission becomes unrestricted.

Core Idea
When the master's authority ends, the ma'dhun's permission becomes irrevocable — majur.

In Hanafi law, a ma'dhun slave has permission to trade, subject to the master's control. But what happens when the master can no longer exercise authority? This passage addresses three scenarios where the master's capacity ends, and the surprising result: the slave's permission actually becomes unrestricted, not revoked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the triggering events
List the three events mentioned in the passage: death, insanity, and apostasy with joining dar al-harb.
These events all terminate the master's ability to exercise control.
2
Understand the ruling 'majur'
The term 'majur' means the permission becomes unrestricted and irrevocable by the master or his heirs.
Contrast with the normal state where the master can revoke permission.
3
Analyze the legal reasoning
The ruling is based on the fact that the master's right to revoke the permission ceases with his death, insanity, or apostasy.
The slave's own permission is not nullified; only the master's supervisory power ends.
4
Contrast with possible misconceptions
Some might assume the permission ends or that the slave becomes free. The Hanafi ruling clarifies the opposite.
The slave remains a slave, but his trading authority continues without restriction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان مات المولى او جن او لحق بدار الحرب فالماذون مجور
[۱۶۸۵] (۱۳) اگر مولی مر گیا یا اس پر جنون طاری ہو گیا یا مرتد ہو کر دار الحرب چلا گیا تو ماذون مجور ہو جائے گا۔
If the master dies, or becomes insane, or joins the abode of war (as an apostate), then the ma'dhun becomes unrestricted (majur). [1685] (13) If the master dies, or becomes insane, or becomes an apostate and goes to dar al-harb, then the ma'dhun becomes majur (free from restriction).
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This ruling deals with what happens when the master can no longer exercise authority. In such cases, the ma'dhun slave's permission to trade is no longer revocable; he becomes 'majur' meaning his authority to trade continues unrestricted. This is because the master's right to control is terminated by death, legal incapacity (insanity), or apostasy (which dissolves the marriage contract and severs ties). The slave's own permission is not affected.

Key Terms
مجور (majūr): Unrestricted; the ma'dhun's permission becomes absolute or continues without possibility of revocation.
دار حرب (dār al-ḥarb): Territory not under Islamic rule; the apostate who joins it is no longer under Muslim law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to a ma'dhun's trade permission when his master dies?
A) The permission is revoked.
B) The permission becomes unrestricted (majur).
C) The permission remains the same.
D) The permission ends and the slave is free.

Key Takeaway
The death, insanity, or apostasy of the master renders the ma'dhun's permission unrestricted (majur), as the master's right to revoke has ended.

Causes of Automatic Restriction for the Ma'dhun Slave
Listing events that automatically restrict the ma'dhun slave and a fiqh dispute on acknowledgment validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the events that automatically restrict the ma'dhun slave and state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of his acknowledgment during restriction.

Core Idea
The ma'dhun slave is automatically restricted when the master loses capacity or the slave absconds.

In Hanafi fiqh, the ma'dhun (permitted) slave enjoys the capacity to trade. However, certain events automatically terminate this permission, making the slave mahjur 'alayh (legally restricted). This lesson enumerates those causes and also examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the validity of the restricted slave's acknowledgment of debts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ma'dhun slave's status
Recognize that the ma'dhun slave is a unique Hanafi category: a slave permitted by his master to engage in trade. His capacity depends on the master's own capacity and permission.
This status is not found in other schools.
2
Extract the causes of automatic restriction
From the text, list the events that automatically restrict the slave: (1) master's apostasy, (2) slave's absconding, (3) master's insanity, (4) master's apostasy and flight to dar al-harb. Each terminates the slave's permission without any formal revocation.
The automatic nature distinguishes these from explicit revocation.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The commentator explains that when the master (the asl) loses the capacity to trade—through apostasy, insanity, or death—his permission to the slave (the far') becomes void, as the branch cannot exist without the root.
This is a core Hanafi usul principle applied in muamalat.
4
Analyze the dispute on acknowledgment
After restriction, the text reports disagreement: Abu Hanifa holds that the slave's acknowledgment of debts regarding wealth in his hand is valid, while the Sahibayn say it is not valid. Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).
The dispute turns on whether the restricted slave still has any capacity to affect his master's property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحرب مرتدا صار الماذون محجورا عليه [۱۲۸۶ (۱۴) ولو ابق العبد الماذون صار محجورا علیه ۱۹۸۷ (۱۵) واذا حجر عليه فاقراره جائز فيما في يده من المال عند ابي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى وقالا لا يصح اقراره [۱۲۸۸ (۱۲) و اذا لزمته ديون تحيط بماله مولی مر گیا تو جو غلام مازون تھا اب وہ تجارت نہیں کر سکے گا مجور ہو جائے گا۔ یا مولی مجنون ہو گیا یا مرتد ہو کر دار الحرب بھاگ گیا اور وہاں مل گیا تو ان صورتوں میں مجبور کرنے کی ضرورت نہیں ۔ غلام خود بخود مجبور ہو جائے گا۔
خود مولی جو اصیل ہے اس میں تجارت کرنے کی اہمیت نہیں رہی تو دوسرے کو تجارت کرنے کی اجازت کیسے دے گا۔ اس لئے مولی پر یہ ایا میں یہ سب حالات طاری ہوتے ہی ماذون مجور ہو جائے گا۔
If the master becomes an apostate, the ma'dhun becomes restricted. [1286 (14)] And if the ma'dhun slave absconds, he becomes restricted. [1987 (15)] And when he is restricted, his acknowledgment regarding the wealth in his hand is valid according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, and they (the two companions) said his acknowledgment is not valid. [1288 (12)] And when debts that surround his wealth become necessary... The master went? So the slave who was ma'dhun now cannot trade; he will become restricted. Or the master became insane, or became an apostate and fled to the abode of war and was found there, then in these cases there is no need to restrict him; the slave becomes restricted automatically. When the master, who is the original, no longer has the capacity to trade, how can he give permission to another to trade? Therefore, when these conditions occur to the master, the ma'dhun becomes restricted.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage lists several events that automatically terminate the permission (idhn) of the ma'dhun slave, making him restricted (mahjur). The principle is that the validity of the slave's permission depends on the master's capacity to trade. If the master loses that capacity due to apostasy, insanity, or death, the slave's permission is automatically revoked. Additionally, if the slave absconds, he becomes restricted because the master can no longer trust him with trade. The commentator explains this using the usul principle that when the original (asl) loses capacity, the branch (far') also loses it.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade.
محجور عليه (mahjur 'alayh): One who is legally restricted from disposing of property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The master becomes an apostate
B) The slave absconds
C) The master designates a new agent for trade
D) The master becomes insane

Key Takeaway
The ma'dhun slave's permission ends automatically when the master loses legal capacity or the slave absconds, with a school dispute on whether the slave's acknowledgment remains valid during restriction.

Ruling on the Ma'dhun Slave Who Absconds
Stating that absconding makes the ma'dhun restricted

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a ma'dhun slave becomes restricted immediately upon absconding and explain the legal reasoning based on loss of trust.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun slave who absconds instantly loses trade permission because trust is broken.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the chapter on the ma'dhun slave: what happens if the slave runs away? The text provides a clear answer grounded in the practical reality of commerce. Trust is the foundation of the permission to trade, and absconding destroys that trust.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the slave absconding (ibaq). The ruling is that he becomes restricted (mahjur), meaning his permission to trade is revoked.
The ruling is immediate, without need for a court order.
2
Understand the legal reasoning
The reason given is loss of trust (i'timad). Neither traders nor the master can trust an absconding slave, so the permission cannot stand.
This is a 'talil' (reasoning) based on custom and practicality.
3
Note the immediacy of the restriction
The restriction takes effect as soon as the slave absconds, not after a grace period or court intervention.
The text says 'as soon as he absconds' (bhāgtē hī).
4
Distinguish from other status changes
Absconding does not cancel ownership of property, only the permission to trade. The slave remains owned by the master.
This is a common point of confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۶۸۶] (۱۴) اگر ماذون غلام بھاگ گیا تو مجور ہو جائے گا۔
ج
بھاگنے والے غلام پر تاجروں کا کیا اعتما در ہے گا ؟ اور خود مولی اس پر تجارت کرنے کا اعتماد کیسے کرے گا ؟ کیونکہ وہ تو مال لیکر ہی غائب ہو
جائے گا۔ اس لئے بھاگنے والا غلام بھاگتے ہی مجور ہو جائے گا۔
[1686] (14) If the ma'dhun slave absconds, he will become restricted. Because what trust will traders have in an absconding slave? And how can the master trust him with trade? Since he will disappear with the wealth. Therefore, the absconding slave becomes restricted as soon as he absconds.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This is a separate ruling stating that if the ma'dhun slave runs away (yabqi), he automatically becomes restricted (mahjur). The reason given is that the trust required for trade is lost; neither traders nor the master can rely on him. The restriction is immediate upon absconding, without need for a court order.

Key Terms
إباق (ibaq): Absconding of a slave
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade
محجور (mahjur): Restricted, legally incapacitated from trading

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) His permission remains valid if he returns within a year
B) He becomes restricted immediately
C) He can still trade if the master agrees
D) He is sold automatically

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun slave who absconds immediately becomes restricted (mahjur) due to loss of trust, with no automatic revival upon return.

Validity of Iqrar After Restriction: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ma'dhun's iqrar after restriction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the validity of a ma'dhun slave's iqrar after restriction and explain the differing bases of possession vs. ownership.

Core Idea
Iqrar hinges on possession, not ownership — Abu Hanifa's view that possession suffices for acknowledgment after restriction.

At the heart of this intra-Hanafi dispute is a fundamental question: does the validity of an acknowledgment (iqrar) depend on legal possession or on ownership? Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the ma'dhun slave's physical possession of property is sufficient to make a binding acknowledgment, even after restriction. The Sahibayn, however, argue that since the property belongs to the master, the restricted slave has no authority to acknowledge anything. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and practical implications of this khilaf.

Guided Path
1
State the factual scenario
A master grants permission (idhn) to his slave to trade (making him ma'dhun), then later restricts (hajr) him. After restriction, the slave makes an iqrar regarding the property still in his physical possession.
The iqrar could be admitting a debt, acknowledging a trust, or confessing usurpation.
2
Identify the two positions
Abu Hanifa says the iqrar is valid; the Sahibayn say it is invalid. State each position clearly.
Both are authoritative Hanafi views; no explicit tarjih in this text.
3
Analyze the basis of each position
For Abu Hanifa, the basis is possession (qabd): the slave has the property in hand, so iqrar is valid. For the Sahibayn, the basis is ownership: the property belongs to the master, so the slave cannot bind it.
Abu Hanifa also adds that the slave may want to clear his liability.
4
Compare the dalil
Abu Hanifa relies on the principle that iqrar is founded on possession, not authority. The Sahibayn argue that restriction removes authority and one cannot acknowledge in another's property.
This is a difference in the effective cause (illa) of iqrar validity.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, under Abu Hanifa the iqrar is binding against the slave (and indirectly the master), while under the Sahibayn it is void. The property in the slave's hand remains subject to the master's ownership until actual third-party claims arise.
The master may challenge the iqrar if it is collusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۶۸۷] (۱۵) اگر حجر کر دے اس پر تو اس کا اقرار جائز ہے اس مال کے بارے میں جو اس کے ہاتھ میں ہے امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک ۔ اور صاحبین نے فرمایا اس کا اقرار صحیح نہیں ہے۔ مولی نے ماذون غلام کو حجر کر دیا۔ اب اس کے قبضے میں جو مال ہے اس کے بارے میں اقرار کرتا ہے کہ یہ مال فلاں کی امانت ہے۔ یا مال فلاں کا غصب کیا ہوا ہے۔ یا مجھ پر فلاں کا اتنا دین ہے اس کے بدلے میں یہ مال دینا ہے تو اس مال کے بارے میں اس قسم کا اقرار کرنا امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک جائز ہے۔ اقرار کا دارو مدار قبضہ ہے۔ اور غلام کا قبضہ اس مال پر ہے اس لئے وہ اقرار کر سکتا ہے (۲) ماذون ابھی ابھی مجبور ہوا ہے اس لئے یہی کہا جا سکتا ہے کہ اس کے ذمے جو لوگوں کے حقوق آتے ہیں ان سے یہ فارغ ہونا چاہتا ہے اس لئے اس کا اقرار درست ہوگا ۔ البتہ جو مال مولی نے لے لیا اور ماذون غلام کے قبضہ میں نہیں رہا اس کے بارے میں کوئی اقرار نہیں کر سکتا۔ انده صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ اب وہ مجبور ہو چکا ہے اس لئے اقرار کرنے کا اختیار اس کو نہیں رہا اس لئے وہ اقرار نہیں کر سکتا۔ کیونکہ جو مال غلام کے ہاتھ میں ہے وہ مولی کا مال ہے اور دوسرے کے مال میں کسی کے لئے اقرار کرنا جائز نہیں ہے۔
[1687] (15) If he restricts him, his acknowledgment is valid regarding the wealth that is in his hand according to Imam Abu Hanifa. And the two companions said his acknowledgment is not valid. The master restricted the ma'dhun slave. Now, regarding the wealth in his possession, he acknowledges: 'This wealth is so-and-so's trust' or 'It is so-and-so's usurped property' or 'I owe so-and-so such amount, and in exchange this wealth must be given' — such acknowledgment is valid according to Abu Hanifa. The basis of acknowledgment is possession. The slave has possession over this wealth, so he can make an acknowledgment. Also, the ma'dhun has just been restricted, so it can be said that he wants to discharge the rights of people that are due from him, so his acknowledgment is correct. However, regarding the wealth that the master has taken and is no longer in the ma'dhun's possession, he cannot make any acknowledgment. The two companions say: He has become restricted, so he no longer has the authority to make an acknowledgment. Therefore, he cannot make an acknowledgment because the wealth in the slave's hand is the master's property, and it is not permissible for anyone to make an acknowledgment regarding another's property.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. The issue: after the ma'dhun slave has been restricted (mahjur), can he make an acknowledgment (iqrar) regarding the property that is physically in his possession? According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the acknowledgment is valid because the validity of iqrar depends on possession (qabd). The slave has the property in his hand, so he can acknowledge it as a trust, a debt, etc. This is also because he may wish to clear his liability. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that since the slave is restricted, he has no authority to make an acknowledgment; the property is owned by the master, and one cannot bind another's property by acknowledgment. Abu Hanifa's view is that possession is the key factor, not ownership.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): A legal acknowledgment or confession regarding property or rights
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who is granted permission by his master to engage in trade
حجر (hajr): Legal restriction or interdiction preventing a person from managing property
قبض (qabd): Physical possession or control over property

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why is the ma'dhun slave's acknowledgment valid after restriction?
A) Because the slave still owns the property
B) Because the basis of iqrar is possession
C) Because the master granted permission again
D) Because the restriction was unlawful

Key Takeaway
The validity of iqrar after restriction depends on whether the basis is possession (Abu Hanifa) or ownership (Sahibayn); both views are authoritative within the Hanafi school.

Effect of Overwhelming Debt on the Ma'dhun Slave's Property
Ruling when the slave's debts exceed his value and property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when the ma'dhun slave's debts exceed his assets, and explain the evidence from the athar.

Core Idea
When debts exceed the slave's property and price, the master loses ownership of the slave's property to creditors.

In this lesson, we examine a specific scenario in the fiqh of ma'dhun (permitted slave trader). If the slave's debts become so large that they cover both his trade capital and his personal market value (raqabah), a shift occurs in ownership. The master can no longer dispose of the property, and the slave may be sold to satisfy creditors. This ruling is supported by an athar from Al-Hakam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for loss of ownership
The ruling applies only when debts 'surround' (ihāṭa) both the slave's property and his person (raqabah). That is, total debt > value of property + market price of the slave.
2
Understand the effect on the master
The master is no longer owner of the slave's property; it effectively belongs to creditors. The slave's person remains the master's property, but its value is considered for debt.
3
Locate the dalil
The author cites an athar from Al-Hakam (a tabi'i) that the ma'dhun slave is not sold unless debt surrounds his neck, then he is sold.
Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, no. 15338
4
Derive the ruling from the dalil
The athar implies that when debt surrounds, sale is permitted. This indicates that the master's ownership has been overridden by creditors' rights.
5
Note the limits of the ruling
The slave's person remains the master's property in principle, but its value is considered for debt coverage. The master can no longer spend the slave's property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۶۸۸) (۱۶) اگر ماذون کو دین لازم ہو جائے جو اس کے مال اور جان کو گھیر لے تو مولی نہیں مالک ہوگا اس کا جو اس کے ہاتھ میں ہے ا ماذون غلام پر اتنا قرض ہو جائے کہ جو مال اس کے ہاتھ میں ہے وہ بھی بک جائے اور خود غلام کو بھی بیچ کر دین ادا کرنا چاہے تو ادا نہ ہو سکے ۔ مثلا غلام اور اس کے مال کی قیمت پانچ ہزار درہم ہیں اور اس پر چھ ہزار قرض ہو گیا ہو تو اب اس کی جان اور مال سب قرض میں گھرا ہوا ہے۔ اور گویا کہ مولی کے غلام کے پاس کچھ بھی نہیں رہا سب قرض خواہوں کا ہو گیا۔ اس لئے مولی اس غلام کے مال کا مالک نہیں رہا۔ اب غلام کے مال کو خرچ کرنا چاہے تو نہیں کر سکتا۔ تاہم غلام ابھی بھی مولی کا ہے اگر مال مولی کا نہیں رہا۔
معنوی طور پر یہ مال اور غلام مازون کی جان قرض والوں کا ہو گیا ہے (۲) اثر میں اس کا اشارہ ہے۔ عن الحكم في العبد الماذون في التجارة قال لا يباع الا ان يحيط الدين برقبته فيباع حينئذ (الف) (مصنف عبد الرزاق، باب هل يباع العبد فی دینہ اذا اذن له اوالحر ؟ ص ۲۸۵ نمبر ۱۵۳۳۸) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ عبد ماذون قرض میں گھر جائے تو بیچا جا سکتا ہے۔ جس سے اندازہ ہوتا ہے کہ غلام اور اس کا مال اب مولی کا نہیں رہا۔
[1688] (16) If a debt becomes necessary for the ma'dhun that surrounds his wealth and his person, then the master will not be the owner of what is in his hand. For example, if the slave and his property are worth five thousand dirhams and he owes six thousand dirhams, then his person and property are all surrounded by debt. It is as if nothing remains with the master's slave; everything has become the creditors'. Therefore, the master is no longer the owner of the slave's property. Now, if he wants to spend the slave's property, he cannot. However, the slave is still the master's property, but the property is no longer the master's. Morally, this property and the ma'dhun slave's person have become the creditors'. There is an indication of this in an athar: Al-Hakam said concerning the ma'dhun slave in trade: 'He is not sold unless the debt surrounds his neck; then he is sold at that time.' (Mussanaf Abd al-Razzaq, Bab hal yuba' al-'abd fi daynihi idha udhina lahu aw al-hurr? p.285 no.15338). From this athar, it is understood that when the ma'dhun is surrounded by debt, he can be sold. This indicates that the slave and his property are no longer the master's.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This ruling deals with the situation where the ma'dhun slave's debts exceed the value of his property and his own price (since he is a slave, his person has value). The principle: when total debts (ma-lihi and raqabah) exceed assets, the master loses ownership of the slave's property, though the slave's person remains the master's in principle. The practical result: the property goes to creditors, and the slave may be sold to pay debts. The author cites an athar from Al-Hakam to support this. The athar states that the ma'dhun slave is not sold unless the debt encompasses his neck; only then is he sold. This implies that the slave's property and person are effectively owned by creditors.

Key Terms
رقبة (raqabah): The slave's person or neck; used as a metonym for the slave's value as property
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who has been granted permission by his master to engage in trade.
إحاطة (ihāṭa): Surrounding; used to describe when debts completely cover (equal or exceed) the slave's assets and person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the effect when the ma'dhun slave's debts exceed the total of his property and his price?
A) The master can still use the slave's property
B) The master loses ownership of the slave's property
C) The slave is automatically freed
D) The debt is cancelled

Key Takeaway
When the ma'dhun's debts exceed the value of his property and his person, the master's ownership of the property transfers to creditors, and the slave may be sold to settle the debt.

Freeing Slaves Purchased by the Ma'dhun: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on master's right to free ma'dhun's slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the master's right to free slaves purchased by the ma'dhun when debts overwhelm, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa blocks the master's manumission of ma'dhun-purchased slaves when debts overwhelm; Sahibayn allow it with liability.

In the previous lesson, we saw that when the ma'dhun's assets are overwhelmed by debts, creditors have priority over his property. Here, a related question arises: can the master (who owns the ma'dhun) free the slaves that the ma'dhun himself purchased? Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn reach different conclusions, rooted in how they view the nature of the master's ownership after debts attach.

Guided Path
1
State the legal scenario
Identify the key elements: the ma'dhun has purchased slaves, his debts exceed his assets, and the master wishes to free those slaves.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Abu Hanifa holds that the master cannot free the slaves because the property is effectively owned by creditors. His proof is the principle of avoiding harm to creditors.
This is a qiyas or istihsan argument.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
The Sahibayn hold that the master can free the slaves, because the property remains legally his, but he becomes liable for the debts. They cite an athar from Al-Zuhri: if a master frees a slave who has debts, the master is liable.
The athar is from Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the master frees the slaves, are they freed? Abu Hanifa says no; Sahibayn say yes, but the master must pay the debts.
5
Consider the tarjih (if any)
The text does not specify which view is mufta bihi; both are presented with evidence. This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۶۸۹) (۱۷) اگر ما زون کے غلاموں کو آزاد کرے تو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک آزاد نہیں ہوں گے۔ اور صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ مولی مالک ہوگا اس چیز کا جو اس کے ہاتھ میں ہے۔
چونکہ ماذون غلام کا مال قرض میں گھر چکا ہے اور گویا کہ مولی اس کے مال کا مالک نہیں رہا اس لئے ماذون غلام نے جو غلام خریدا ہے اس غلام کو مولی آزاد کرنا چاہے تو نہیں کر سکتا۔ یہ غلام گویا کہ مولی کے نہیں رہے بلکہ قرض والوں کے ہو گئے اس لئے مولی مازون غلام کے غلاموں کو آزاد کرے تو آزاد نہیں ہوں گے صول اس اصول پر ہے کہ قرض والوں کو نقصان نہ ہو۔ صاحبین فرماتے ہیں ماذون غلام کے مال اور جان چاہے قرض میں گھر چکے ہوں پھر بھی وہ مولی کا مال ہے اس لئے مولی اس کے مال کو استعمال کرنا چاہے تو کر سکتا ہے۔ اسی طرح ماذون غلام کے خریدے ہوئے غلاموں کو آزاد کرنا چاہے تو آزاد کر سکتا ہے۔ البتہ اس صورت میں مولی قرض خواہوں کے قرضوں کا ذمہ دار ہو جائے گا۔
چاہے مال اور جان قرض میں گھر گئے ہوں پھر بھی وہ مولی کا مال ہے اس لئے مولی اس کے مال کو استعمال بھی کر سکتا ہے اور اس کے خریدے ہوئے غلام کو آزاد بھی کر سکتا ہے (۲) اثر میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ عن الزهري قال اذا اعتق الرجل عبده وعليه دين فالدين على السيد (ب) مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب هل يباع العبد فی دینہ اذا اذن له او الحرص ۲۸۶ نمبر ۱۵۲۴۲) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ مولی غلام کو آزاد کرنا چاہے تو کر سکتا ہے۔ البتہ اس کا قرض مولی کے ذمے ہو جائے گا۔
[1689] (17) If he frees the ma'dhun's slaves, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa they will not be freed. And the two companions say that the master will be the owner of what is in his hand. Since the ma'dhun's property has been surrounded by debt, and the master is no longer the owner of his property, therefore, if the ma'dhun has purchased slaves, the master cannot free them if he wants. These slaves are no longer the master's; they have become the creditors'. Therefore, if the master frees the ma'dhun's slaves, they will not be freed, based on the principle that creditors should not be harmed. The two companions say: Even if the ma'dhun's property and person have been surrounded by debt, it is still the master's property. Therefore, the master can use his property as he wishes. Similarly, he can free the slaves purchased by the ma'dhun. However, in this case, the master becomes responsible for the creditors' debts. There is evidence for this in an athar: Al-Zuhri said: 'If a man frees his slave and he has a debt, the debt is upon the master.' (Mussanaf Abd al-Razzaq, Bab hal yuba' al-'abd fi daynihi... p.286 no.15242). From this athar, it is understood that the master can free the slave, but the debt becomes his responsibility.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage presents another intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. The scenario: the ma'dhun slave has purchased other slaves (using his trade capital). If the ma'dhun's debts have overwhelmed his assets (as in the previous ruling), can the master free those purchased slaves? Abu Hanifa says no, because the property is effectively owned by creditors; freeing them would harm creditors' rights. The Sahibayn say yes, because the property remains legally the master's; he can free them, but he becomes liable for the debts of the ma'dhun, effectively compensating the creditors. They cite an athar from Al-Zuhri that if a master frees a slave who has debts, the master is liable. This supports their view.

Key Terms
عتق (itq): Manumission or freeing of a slave; requires ownership.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade with capital; also refers to the slave himself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why can the master not free the slaves purchased by the ma'dhun when debts overwhelm?
A) Because those slaves are owned by the creditors
B) Because the master has no right to free any slave
C) Because the ma'dhun has the right to free them
D) Because the slaves have already been freed by the debt

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn disagree on whether the master's freeing of ma'dhun-purchased slaves is valid when debts overwhelm, with the key difference being the master's liability for debts.
